Fourth Argument: Disgrace and Hell
Razi's fourth argument: disgrace only for disbelievers => Hell only for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses two Quranic verses to argue that only disbelievers will enter Hell, based on the concept of disgrace.

Core Idea
Disgrace is exclusive to disbelievers, and Hell entails disgrace.

Imam Razi presents a concise syllogism grounded in Quranic verses. He establishes that disgrace (al-khizy) is a punishment specifically for disbelievers. Then, through another verse, he shows that entering Hell necessarily involves disgrace. Therefore, Hell must be reserved for disbelievers alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the two verses
Read the Arabic verses from Surah al-Nahl and Surah Aal-e-Imran. Notice how they are used as premises.
The first verse emphasizes disgrace upon disbelievers; the second links Hell to disgrace.
2
Understand the logical connection
Identify the major premise (disgrace for disbelievers) and minor premise (Hell brings disgrace) to reach the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is important for the doctrine of the afterlife.
This argument reinforces that believers will not be eternally punished.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحجة الرابعة : إِنَّ الْخِزْيَ الْيَوْمَ وَالسُّوءَ عَلَى الْكَافِرِينَ [النحل: 27] دلت هذه الآية على اختصاص الخزي بالكافرين، ثم إن كل من دخل النار فقد حصل له الخزي لقوله تعالى : ﴿رَبَّنَا إِنَّكَ مَن تُدْخِلِ النَّارَ فَقَدْ أَخْزَيْتَهُ﴾ [آل عمران: 192] ولما لم يحصل الخزي إلا للكافرين وجب أن لا يحصل دخول النار إلا لهم.
And the fourth proof: 'Indeed, disgrace today and evil are upon the disbelievers' [al-Nahl 16:27]. This verse indicates that disgrace is specific to disbelievers. Then, everyone who enters Hell has certainly incurred disgrace, due to His saying, the Most High: 'Our Lord, indeed whoever You admit to the Fire, You have disgraced him' [Aal-e-Imran 3:192]. Since disgrace is not incurred except by disbelievers, it follows that entering the Fire does not occur except for them.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
Razi combines two verses: one states that disgrace (al-khizy) is exclusively for disbelievers; another states that anyone admitted to Hell is disgraced. Therefore, only disbelievers can enter Hell, because if a believer entered Hell, he would be disgraced, but disgrace is only for disbelievers. This is a syllogism: major premise – disgrace is only for disbelievers; minor premise – entering Hell entails disgrace; conclusion – entering Hell is only for disbelievers.

Key Terms
الخزي
السوء
الكافرون
دخول النار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believers who enter Hell temporarily might also be disgraced.
Correction: The Qur'an states that disgrace is specifically upon disbelievers (Surah al-Nahl 16:27). Since entering Hell entails disgrace, believers cannot enter Hell because that would contradict the exclusivity of disgrace.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what is the relationship between entering Hell and disgrace?
A) Entering Hell causes disgrace
B) Disgrace causes entering Hell
C) They are unrelated
D) Entering Hell prevents disgrace

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the exclusive nature of disgrace and the disgrace of Hell to prove that only disbelievers will enter Hell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Disgrace is exclusive to disbelievers. When you sin, do you ever feel a sense of disgrace that should drive you to repent, or do you become comfortable with it? How does this verse from al-Nahl make you reconsider your own state of faith?
?
The believers' prayer in Aal-e-Imran acknowledges that anyone admitted to the Fire has been disgraced. Do you truly fear that disgrace enough to avoid sins that could lead to Hell? What specific habit in your life right now contradicts this fear?
?
Razi uses logic to prove that believers will never enter Hell. Is your heart at peace with this guarantee, or does it make you complacent? How should this assurance change how you live today?

Fifth Argument: Forgiveness of All Sins Without Repentance
Razi argues from a general verse that forgiveness can be without repentance, then addresses an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the verse 'Forgive all sins' to argue that forgiveness can occur without repentance, and how he addresses the objection that this includes disbelief.

Core Idea
A general verse can imply forgiveness without repentance, but it may be specified by stronger texts.

Razi begins his fifth argument by citing a powerful verse that promises forgiveness for all sins without any condition of repentance. An immediate objection arises: does this mean even disbelief is forgiven? Razi's response shows the sophistication of theological reasoning when general verses meet specific ones.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its implication
Read the Arabic of verse 39:53 and its translation. Notice that it says 'forgives all sins' without mentioning repentance. Ask yourself: what does this imply about the necessity of repentance?
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that if the verse covers all sins, it would include disbelief (kufr). But we know from other texts that disbelief is not forgiven without repentance. How can Razi reconcile this?
3
Follow Razi's response
Razi gives two responses: (1) the general statement can be specified by other evidence, leaving the rest valid; (2) the word 'servants' in the verse is often limited to believers, so the verse applies to believers only.
Notice how Razi uses linguistic and contextual arguments.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument supports the possibility of forgiveness without repentance for sins other than shirk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا عِبَادِيَ الَّذِينَ أَسْرَفُوا عَلَى أَنفُسِهِمْ لَا تَقْنَطُوا مِن رَحْمَةِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعاً﴾ [الزمر: 53] حكم بغفران الذنوب جميعا، ولم يشترط فيه التوبة وذلك يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر الذنوب جميعا قبل التوبة وبعدها، فإن قيل : فيلزم من دلالتها مغفرة الكفر لأنه من الذنوب قلنا هب أن هذا العام دخله هذا : التخصيص، فيبقى فيما عداه حجة، وأيضًا لفظ العباد في القرآن مخصوص بالمؤمنين، قال تعالى : ﴿عَيْنًا يَشْرَبُ بِهَا عِبَادُ اللَّهِ﴾ [الإنسان: 6] الحجة السادسة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ رَبَّكَ لَذُو مَغْفِرَةٍ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى ظُلْمِهِمْ﴾ [الرعد: 6] وكلمة "على" تفيد الحال كقولك : رأيت الأمير على أكله، أي رأيته حال أكله، فكذا ههنا دلت الآية على أنه تعالى يغفر لهم حال اشتغالهم بالظلم، وحال اشتغالهم بالظلم يمنع حصول التوبة، فعلمنا أنه يحصل الغفران بدون التوبة، فيقتضي هذا الدليل حصول الغفران للمشرك ؛ لأن الشرك ظلم، بدليل قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ الشِّرْكَ لَظُلْمٌ عَظِيمٌ﴾ [لقمان: 13] إلا أنه ترك العمل به لقوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ﴾ [النساء: 48] فيبقى حجة فيما عداه، والفرق أن الكفر أعظم حالا من المعصية.
The fifth proof: His saying, the Most High: 'O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of Allah. Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' [al-Zumar 39:53]. It rules forgiveness of all sins and does not stipulate repentance, and that indicates that He forgives all sins before repentance and after it. If it is said: This would entail forgiveness of disbelief because it is among the sins. We say: Even if this general statement includes this specification, it remains a proof for what is beyond it. Also, the word 'servants' in the Qur'an is specific to believers, as He said: 'A spring from which the servants of Allah drink' [al-Insan 76:6]. The sixth proof: His saying, the Most High: 'And indeed, your Lord is full of forgiveness for the people despite their wrongdoing' [al-Ra'd 13:6]. The word 'on' (على) indicates accompaniment, like your saying: I saw the prince while he was eating, i.e., I saw him in the state of his eating. So likewise here, the verse indicates that He forgives them while they are engaged in wrongdoing, and being engaged in wrongdoing prevents the occurrence of repentance. Thus we know that forgiveness occurs without repentance. This proof necessitates forgiveness for the polytheist because polytheism is wrongdoing, as per His saying: 'Indeed, polytheism is a great wrongdoing' [Luqman 31:13]. However, it is not acted upon due to His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [al-Nisa 4:48]. So it remains a proof for what is beyond that. The difference is that disbelief is a greater state than disobedience.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage combines two closely related arguments. In the fifth argument, Razi cites the verse that says Allah forgives all sins without stipulating repentance, which implies that forgiveness can occur without repentance. An objection is raised: this would include forgiveness of disbelief. Razi answers that this general statement may be specified (excluding disbelief) by other texts (like 4:48), but remains valid for lesser sins. He also notes that the word 'servants' in the verse is often specific to believers, as in 76:6. In the sixth argument, Razi uses the verse where forgiveness is given 'on' (على) their wrongdoing, which he interprets as 'while' they are doing wrong, meaning forgiveness is granted at the very moment of sin, which precludes repentance. Thus, repentance is not required. Again, this would even include polytheists, but that is excluded by the specific verse 4:48. Razi concludes that the argument remains valid for sins other than shirk. The final line notes the distinction: disbelief is a more severe state than mere disobedience.

Key Terms
الغفران
التوبة
الذنوب
العموم
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'forgives all sins' means that everyone, including disbelievers, is forgiven without any condition.
Correction: Razi shows that the general statement is specified by other texts (like 4:48) and the address is limited to believers, so it does not apply to shirk.

Checkpoint
What does Razi use to argue that the verse 'forgives all sins' does not include disbelief?
A) The word 'servants' is specific to believers
B) The verse is abrogated
C) Repentance is a condition in all verses
D) Disbelief is not a sin

Key Takeaway
A general verse can imply forgiveness without repentance, but it must be interpreted in light of other texts and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have heard that Allah forgives all sins. But have you ever knowingly delayed repentance, assuming His mercy will cover you regardless? Is that not a dangerous presumption that this verse warns against when properly understood? Will you commit to repenting promptly, without relying on the general wording alone?
?
When you read 'O My servants who have transgressed against themselves', do you see yourself as that servant? And if so, does the verse give you comfort or does it demand that you not despair? But what if you are not truly His servant in the sense of belief? Examine your heart: are you among those addressed? If not, what must you change?
?
Razi shows that a general verse can be limited by other texts. How often do you cherry-pick verses that suit your desires while ignoring the clarifying ones? Will you commit to reading the Quran holistically, not selectively, and accept its full guidance even when it challenges your assumptions?

Sixth Argument: Forgiveness During Wrongdoing
Razi interprets a preposition to argue that forgiveness occurs during the sin, thus without repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi interprets the word 'on' in verse 13:6 to mean accompaniment, proving that forgiveness can occur while one is sinning, thus without repentance.

Core Idea
The word 'on' can indicate simultaneous action, showing forgiveness without repentance.

Razi's sixth argument uses a subtle linguistic point: the preposition 'on' (على) in the verse about forgiveness for wrongdoing indicates the state of doing wrong. This suggests forgiveness is granted at the very moment of sin, making repentance impossible at that moment. Again, Razi must address the problem of polytheism.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and its key word
Read verse 13:6: 'Your Lord is full of forgiveness for the people despite their wrongdoing.' Focus on the word 'on' (على). Razi interprets it as meaning 'while' or 'in the state of'.
Compare with the example: 'I saw the prince on his eating' meaning 'while eating'.
2
Understand the implication
If forgiveness happens while they are doing wrong, then they are not repenting at that moment. Thus forgiveness is without repentance.
3
Follow the objection and response
The objection: this would include polytheists because shirk is wrongdoing. Razi concedes this, but says it is excluded by the explicit verse 4:48. So the argument stands for other sins.
Razi also notes the difference: disbelief is a greater state than disobedience.
4
Reflect on the method
Observe how Razi uses linguistic analysis (the meaning of 'على') to derive theological conclusions. This is a hallmark of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا عِبَادِيَ الَّذِينَ أَسْرَفُوا عَلَى أَنفُسِهِمْ لَا تَقْنَطُوا مِن رَحْمَةِ اللَّهِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعاً﴾ [الزمر: 53] حكم بغفران الذنوب جميعا، ولم يشترط فيه التوبة وذلك يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر الذنوب جميعا قبل التوبة وبعدها، فإن قيل : فيلزم من دلالتها مغفرة الكفر لأنه من الذنوب قلنا هب أن هذا العام دخله هذا : التخصيص، فيبقى فيما عداه حجة، وأيضًا لفظ العباد في القرآن مخصوص بالمؤمنين، قال تعالى : ﴿عَيْنًا يَشْرَبُ بِهَا عِبَادُ اللَّهِ﴾ [الإنسان: 6] الحجة السادسة : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنَّ رَبَّكَ لَذُو مَغْفِرَةٍ لِلنَّاسِ عَلَى ظُلْمِهِمْ﴾ [الرعد: 6] وكلمة "على" تفيد الحال كقولك : رأيت الأمير على أكله، أي رأيته حال أكله، فكذا ههنا دلت الآية على أنه تعالى يغفر لهم حال اشتغالهم بالظلم، وحال اشتغالهم بالظلم يمنع حصول التوبة، فعلمنا أنه يحصل الغفران بدون التوبة، فيقتضي هذا الدليل حصول الغفران للمشرك ؛ لأن الشرك ظلم، بدليل قوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ الشِّرْكَ لَظُلْمٌ عَظِيمٌ﴾ [لقمان: 13] إلا أنه ترك العمل به لقوله تعالى : ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ﴾ [النساء: 48] فيبقى حجة فيما عداه، والفرق أن الكفر أعظم حالا من المعصية.
The fifth proof: His saying, the Most High: 'O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of Allah. Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' [al-Zumar 39:53]. It rules forgiveness of all sins and does not stipulate repentance, and that indicates that He forgives all sins before repentance and after it. If it is said: This would entail forgiveness of disbelief because it is among the sins. We say: Even if this general statement includes this specification, it remains a proof for what is beyond it. Also, the word 'servants' in the Qur'an is specific to believers, as He said: 'A spring from which the servants of Allah drink' [al-Insan 76:6]. The sixth proof: His saying, the Most High: 'And indeed, your Lord is full of forgiveness for the people despite their wrongdoing' [al-Ra'd 13:6]. The word 'on' (على) indicates accompaniment, like your saying: I saw the prince while he was eating, i.e., I saw him in the state of his eating. So likewise here, the verse indicates that He forgives them while they are engaged in wrongdoing, and being engaged in wrongdoing prevents the occurrence of repentance. Thus we know that forgiveness occurs without repentance. However, it is not acted upon due to His saying: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [al-Nisa 4:48]. So it remains a proof for what is beyond that. ?

?

?
The argument excludes shirk. Do you treat other sins lightly, thinking they will be forgiven anyway, while ignoring the severity of even a single act of disobedience that challenges His lordship? What would it take for you to truly hate the sin you are currently committing?

Seventh Argument: Recompense Only for the Ungrateful
Razi argues that punishment is only for extreme disbelievers using an intensive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that divine recompense (punishment) is reserved for the extreme disbeliever (original disbeliever), and how he resolves apparent conflict with other verses.

Core Idea
Recompense is only for the extreme ingrate, not the sinful believer.

Razi presents a proof based on a Qur'anic verse that uses an intensive form, restricting punishment to the most severe category of disbelief. He then anticipates an objection from another verse that seems more universal. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and his principle of giving precedence to forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse (Saba 34:17) and its translation. Notice the word 'الكفور' (al-kafur).
This is the proof text for Razi's argument.
2
Understand the inference from the intensive form
Razi argues that the intensive form 'كفور' (extremely ungrateful) limits the ruling to the original disbeliever. Consider why this linguistic point is important.
The intensive form indicates a heightened degree of ingratitude.
3
Engage with the objection
Read the objection: the verse 'Whoever does evil will be recompensed for it' (4:123) seems to contradict. Reflect on the apparent conflict.
This objection challenges the specificity of the first verse.
4
Learn Razi's resolution
Razi's response: when verses conflict, priority is given to forgiveness (the more merciful interpretation). This is a principle in dealing with apparent contradictions.
This resolution shows the methodology of balancing textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهَلْ يُجَازَى إِلَّا الْكَفُورَ﴾ [سبأ: 17] ولفظ الكفور للمبالغة، فوجب أن يختص هذا الحكم بالكافر الأصلي، لا يقال : هذا معارض بقوله تعالى: ﴿مَن يَعْمَلْ سُوءًا يُجْزَ بِهِ﴾ [النساء: 123] لأنا نقول : لما وقع التعارض بين العفو والوعيد، كان الرجحان لجانب العفو - على ما سيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى في وجوه الترجيحات.
The seventh proof: His saying, the Most High: 'And how can there be recompense except for the ungrateful?' [Saba 34:17]. The word 'kafur' (الْكَفُورَ) indicates intensiveness, so it follows that this ruling is specific to the original disbeliever (al-kafir al-asli). It cannot be said that this is contradicted by His saying, the Most High: 'Whoever does evil will be recompensed for it' [al-Nisa 4:123]. For we say: When there is a conflict between forgiveness and threat, the preponderance is given to the side of forgiveness – as will come, Allah willing, in the chapters on preponderances.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
Razi cites a verse that uses the intensive form 'al-kafur' (extreme ingrate), arguing that such a term is limited to the original disbeliever who persists in ingratitude, not to a sinful believer. He then anticipates an objection: another verse (4:123) seems to say that anyone who does evil will be recompensed. Razi responds that when two verses conflict, one promising forgiveness (or limiting punishment) and another threatening punishment, the side of forgiveness is given precedence. This is a general principle in dealing with apparent contradictions.

Key Terms
الكفور
المبالغة
الكافر الأصلي
الترجيحات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'kafur' applies to any sinner or disbeliever, but Razi restricts it to the original disbeliever due to the intensive form.
Correction: The term 'kafur' is an intensive form, so it refers to extreme ingratitude, i.e., the original disbeliever who persists in disbelief.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi restrict the verse to the original disbeliever?
A) Because it uses an intensive form
B) Because it is abrogated
C) Because it appears in a Meccan sura
D) Because it refers to the afterlife only

Key Takeaway
Razi limits the threat of punishment to the original disbeliever based on the intensive form 'kafur', and resolves conflicting verses by prioritizing divine forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that Allah's forgiveness outweighs His threat of punishment, or do you live in subtle fear that every misdeed will be requited? How does Razi's principle of giving preponderance to 'afw over wa'id challenge your daily trust in divine mercy? What concrete act of reliance on Allah's forgiveness can you perform today – perhaps sincerely repenting for a specific sin you have been avoiding?

Eighth Argument: Black Faces for Apostates Only
Razi argues that only apostates get black faces and punishment, using specification to address the objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 3:106 to argue that only apostates receive black faces and punishment, and how he applies the principle of specification (takhsis) to address the objection about original disbelievers.

Core Idea
Black faces and punishment are for apostates, not original disbelievers.

Razi presents a proof from the Quran (3:106) where Allah distinguishes two groups on Judgment Day: those with white faces and those with black faces. The black-faced are directly addressed as having disbelieved after faith, implying they were believers first. This raises a question about original disbelievers, and Razi resolves it through the concept of specification (takhsis). In this lesson, we will explore how this argument supports the Sunni position regarding the fate of sinful believers and the specificity of the verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the verse citation and Razi's response to the objection.
2
Identify the two groups in the verse
Notice that the verse mentions two categories: those whose faces become white and those whose faces become black. The black-faced are addressed with 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?'.
This indicates that the black-faced had faith before disbelieving—they are apostates.
3
Understand the problem
Recognize the potential issue: if only apostates are punished, what about original disbelievers? Razi acknowledges this objection.
The objection is that the verse seems to exclude original disbelievers from punishment, which contradicts other proofs.
4
Learn Razi's solution: specification
Razi replies that the generality of the verse is specified to this particular case (apostates), so it does not negate punishment for original disbelievers, who are punished by other evidence.
This is an application of the usul al-fiqh principle of takhsis (specification).
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this argument matters: it helps defend the idea that sinful believers are not punished with lasting punishment, while original disbelievers are punished through other texts.
This argument is part of a larger debate about the fate of grave sinners (murtaqib al-kabira).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : أنه تعالى جعل المكلفين صنفين، فقال: ﴿يَوْمَ تَبْيَضُ وُجُوهٌ وَتَسْوَدُ وُجُوهٌ فَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ اسْوَدَّتْ وُجُوهُهُمْ أَكَفَرْتُم بَعْدَ إِيمَانِكُمْ فَذُوقُوا الْعَذَابَ بِمَا كُنتُمْ تَكْفُرُونَ﴾ [آل عمران: 106] فجعل كل من اسودت وجوههم من الذين كفروا بعد إيمانهم من أهل العذاب وجعل من ابيضت وجوههم من أهل الثواب وأقصى ما في الباب أن يقال : يشكل هذا بالكافر الأصلي إلا أنا نقول خص العموم في هذه الصورة، فيبقى حجة فيما سواها.
The eighth proof: That He, the Most High, made the morally responsible people into two categories, saying: 'On the Day when faces become white and faces become black. As for those whose faces become black, 'Did you disbelieve after your faith? Then taste the punishment for what you used to disbelieve.' [Aal-e-Imran 3:106]. Thus He made everyone whose faces become black among those who disbelieved after their faith to be among the people of punishment, and He made those whose faces become white among the people of reward. The most that can be said is that this is problematic regarding the original disbeliever, but we say: the generality is specified in this case, so it remains a proof for what is beyond it.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
Razi uses the verse that distinguishes two groups on Judgment Day: those with white faces and those with black faces. The black-faced are addressed: 'Did you disbelieve after your faith?' This shows they had faith before, so they are apostates, not original disbelievers. Therefore, the punishment (Hell) is for those who disbelieved after faith, not for original disbelievers? But Razi acknowledges that this seems to exclude original disbelievers from punishment, which is not intended. He explains that the verse's generality is specified to this particular case (apostates), and it remains a valid argument for excluding sinful believers (who did not apostatize) from Hell.

Key Terms
ابيضاض الوجوه
اسوداد الوجوه
الكفر بعد الإيمان
التخصيص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse about black faces implies that original disbelievers are not punished.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the verse is specific to apostates; original disbelievers are punished through other proofs, and the verse's generality is specified.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, who are the people with black faces?
A) All disbelievers
B) Those who disbelieved after faith
C) All sinners
D) Those who never believed

Key Takeaway
Razi's eighth argument shows that only those who disbelieve after belief are addressed with black faces and punishment, and the verse's generality is specified, preserving punishment for original disbelievers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Bright Faces vs Dusty Faces: The Ninth Proof
Razi cites Quranic verse to argue for punishment of disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the description of faces on the Day of Judgment to argue that only disbelievers will be punished in Hell.

Core Idea
Dusty faces signal eternal disgrace for the disbelievers.

In this ninth proof, Razi turns to another vivid description from the Qur'an about the Day of Judgment: the contrast between bright, laughing faces and dusty, darkened faces. The verse explicitly labels the dust-faced as 'the disbelievers, the wicked,' connecting their external state to their eternal fate. This lesson unpacks that connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and translation
Read the Arabic verse and its English translation carefully. Notice the two contrasting descriptions of faces.
The verse is from Surah Abasa, verses 38-42.
2
Identify the two groups
The verse first describes bright and laughing faces, then dusty and darkened faces. It then identifies the latter as 'the disbelievers, the wicked.' This is the key link between appearance and identity.
3
Understand the argument
Razi argues that because the dusty-faced are explicitly called disbelievers, only they will suffer the punishment described in Hell. The bright-faced are implied to be believers who will be rewarded.
Razi mentions the reasoning is similar to his second proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة قوله تعالى: ﴿وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ مُّسْفِرَةٌ ضَاحِكَةٌ مُّسْتَبْشِرَةٌ وَوُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ عَلَيْهَا غَبَرَةٌ تَرْهَقُهَا قَتَرَةٌ أُولَئِكَ هُمُ الْكَفَرَةُ الْفَجَرَةُ﴾ [عبس: 38-42]. وجه الاستدلال بهذه الآية كما تقدم في الحجة الثانية.
The ninth proof: His saying, the Most High: 'Faces that Day will be bright, laughing, rejoicing. And faces that Day will have upon them dust, overcast with darkness. Those are the disbelievers, the wicked.' [Abasa 80:38-42]. The manner of reasoning with this verse is as previously mentioned in the second proof.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
Razi cites the verse describing two types of faces on the Day of Judgment: bright and laughing (believers), and dusty and darkened (disbelievers and wicked). Since the dusty-faced are explicitly identified as 'the disbelievers, the wicked,' this shows that only such people will be in a state of disgrace, implying they are the inhabitants of Hell. The reasoning is similar to the second proof: only those who are described as disbelievers will face punishment.

Razi's Tenth Argument: Hell is Only for Deniers of Resurrection
Razi argues that Hell is only for deniers of the resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that only those who deny the resurrection are condemned to Hell, based on the Quranic classification of mankind.

Core Idea
Hell is exclusively for those who deny the resurrection.

Razi presents a logical argument based on the Quran's threefold division of humanity into the foremost, companions of the right, and companions of the left. He shows that only the companions of the left are in Hell, and they are defined as those who deny the resurrection. This argument carries implications for the fate of sinful believers, distinguishing them from deniers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get a feel for the argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure you understand each part.
Notice the Quranic verse quoted in the passage.
2
Identify the three groups and their fates
Razi lists three categories: السابقون, أصحاب الميمنة, أصحاب المشأمة. Note that the first two are in Paradise, the third in Hell.
3
Trace the logical deduction
Follow the reasoning: (1) Hell's inhabitants are only companions of the left. (2) Companions of the left are those who deny resurrection. Therefore, Hell's inhabitants are only deniers of resurrection.
This is a syllogism: A = B, B = C, so A = C.
4
Consider the final remark
Razi notes that this argument is not applied to all disbelievers (ترك العمل به في حق سائر الكفار) but remains a proof for sinners (الفساق). Think about what this means for believers who sin.
This suggests that sinful believers are not condemned to eternal Hell, unlike those who deny resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : أنه تعالى قسم المكلفين إلى ثلاثة أقسام السابقون وأصحاب الميمنة، وأصحاب المشأمة، ثم ذكر أن السابقين وأصحاب الميمنة في الجنة، وأن أصحاب المشأمة في النار ثم بين أن أصحاب المشأمة الذين يقولون : ﴿وَقَالُوا أَئِذَا مِتْنَا وَكُنَّا تُرَابًا وَعِظَامًا أَئِنَّا لَمَبْعُوثُونَ﴾ [المؤمنون: 82] ولما ثبت أن أصحاب النار ليسوا إلا أصحاب المشأمة وثبت أن أصحاب المشأمة الذين ينكرون البعث وجب أن يقال: إن أصحاب النار ليسوا إلا الذين ينكرون البعث. ترك العمل به في حق سائر الكفار فيبقى حجة في الفساق.
The tenth proof: That He, the Most High, divided the morally responsible into three categories: the foremost, the companions of the right, and the companions of the left. Then He mentioned that the foremost and the companions of the right are in Paradise, and that the companions of the left are in the Fire. Then He explained that the companions of the left are those who say: 'And they say, 'When we have died and become dust and bones, will we indeed be resurrected?'' [al-Mu'minun 23:82]. And since it is established that the inhabitants of the Fire are only the companions of the left, and it is established that the companions of the left are those who deny the resurrection, it follows that the inhabitants of the Fire are only those who deny the resurrection. It is not acted upon with regard to other disbelievers, so it remains a proof for the sinful (fussaq).
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
Razi uses the classification from Surah al-Waqi'ah (though he references verses from al-Mu'minun) to argue that only those who deny the resurrection (i.e., the companions of the left) are in Hell. The foremost and companions of the right are in Paradise. Since deniers of the resurrection are specifically the companions of the left, and only they enter Hell, it follows that Hell is exclusively for those who deny the resurrection. This excludes believers who sin but do not deny resurrection. Razi notes that this argument is not applied to all disbelievers (perhaps because some disbelievers might not deny resurrection in the same way? He says 'ترك العمل به في حق سائر الكفار' meaning it is set aside for other disbelievers, but remains a proof for sinners (meaning sinful believers are not included in Hell).

Key Terms
السابقون
أصحاب الميمنة
أصحاب المشأمة
إنكار البعث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that this argument condemns all disbelievers to Hell.
Correction: Razi specifically limits the conclusion to those who deny resurrection. He says it is not acted upon regarding other disbelievers, meaning disbelievers who do not deny resurrection may be treated differently.

Checkpoint
Which group does Razi identify as the inhabitants of Hell?
A) The foremost
B) The companions of the right
C) The companions of the left
D) All three groups

Key Takeaway
Razi uses the Quranic threefold division to prove that Hell is exclusively for those who deny the resurrection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you truly live with the certainty that you will be resurrected? How would your daily choices change if you genuinely believed that you will stand before God and be held accountable?

Razi's argument uses logic to deduce that Hell is only for those who deny the resurrection. Does your faith in the afterlife rest on such rational conviction, or is it merely inherited tradition? What would it take for you to move from inherited belief to personal certainty?
?
If the resurrection is real, then every sin you commit today is a preparation for that day. Are you preparing, or are you ignoring the inevitable? What is one specific habit you can change today to align your life with the reality of the Hereafter?

Razi's Proof That the Grave Sinner Does Not Enter the Fire
Razi argues that the grave sinner is a believer and will not be disgraced, hence not enter the fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's deductive argument from Quranic verses that a grave sinner, being a believer, will not be disgraced or enter the Fire.

Core Idea
The grave sinner’s faith guarantees freedom from disgrace and the Fire.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents a tightly reasoned proof for the Ash'ari position on the fate of grave sinners. He uses three Quranic verses to establish two premises, from which he deduces that no believer—including the grave sinner—will ever enter the Fire. Let's examine the logical chain step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two key premises
Razi first cites two verses to establish that the grave sinner is a believer and that the believer is not disgraced. Find these two premises in the text.
The verses are from Surat al-Tahrim and Surat Aal 'Imran.
3
Understand the link between fire and disgrace
Razi then cites a third verse equating entry into the Fire with disgrace. See how this connects to the previous premises.
This verse is from Surat Aal 'Imran, verse 192.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
Combine the premises: if the grave sinner is a believer and believers are not disgraced, then the grave sinner is not disgraced; and since entering the Fire is disgrace, the grave sinner cannot enter the Fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون: تعالى: ﴿يَوْمَ لَا يُخْزِي اللهُ النَّبِيَّ وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مَعَهُ﴾ [التحريم: ٨] ولقوله تعالى حكاية عن المؤمنين: ﴿وَلَا تُخْزِنَا يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٩٤] ثم قال تعالى: ﴿فَاسْتَجَابَ رَبُّهُمْ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٩٥] فثبت أن صاحب الكبيرة مؤمن، وثبت أن المؤمن لا يخزى، فيلزم أن صاحب الكبيرة لا يخزى، وإنما قلنا إن كل من أدخل النار فقد أخزي لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّكَ مَن تُدْخِلِ النَّارَ فَقَدْ أَخْزَيْتَهُ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٩٢] ولما ثبتت هاتان المقدمتان لزم القطع بأن صاحب الكبيرة لا يدخل النار.
The thirty-fifth issue: He, exalted, says: {The day when God will not disgrace the Prophet and those who believed with him} [al-Tahrim: 8]. And by His saying, exalted, narrating from the believers: {And do not disgrace us on the Day of Resurrection} [Aal 'Imran: 194]. Then He, exalted, said: {So their Lord responded to them} [Aal 'Imran: 195]. So it is established that the committer of a major sin is a believer, and it is established that the believer is not disgraced, so it necessarily follows that the committer of a major sin is not disgraced. And we only said that everyone who enters the fire has been disgraced because of His saying, exalted: {Indeed, whoever You admit into the fire, You have disgraced him} [Aal 'Imran: 192]. And when these two premises are established, the certainty follows that the committer of a major sin does not enter the fire.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (hujja) from the Ash'ari position on the fate of grave sinners. The author first cites two verses that promise that believers will not be disgraced on the Day of Resurrection: one about the Prophet and those with him, and another where the believers ask not to be disgraced, with God's response. From these he establishes two premises: (1) the committer of a major sin is a believer, and (2) the believer is not disgraced. Then he introduces a third verse that equates entering the fire with disgrace. Combining these, he concludes that the grave sinner will not enter the fire, because if he did, he would be disgraced, contradicting the premise that believers are not disgraced. The argument is deductive: if the grave sinner is a believer and believers are not disgraced, then the grave sinner cannot be disgraced, and since entering fire is disgrace, he cannot enter the fire.

Key Terms
صاحب الكبيرة
يخزى
النار
المقدمتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا يخزى' applies only to a specific group and not to all believers.
Correction: The verse promises no disgrace for the Prophet and those who believed with him, and the believers' prayer for no disgrace shows it applies to all believers, including grave sinners.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what does the verse 'إِنَّكَ مَن تُدْخِلِ النَّارَ فَقَدْ أَخْزَيْتَهُ' establish?
A) That entering the fire is a form of disgrace.
B) That the grave sinner is a believer.
C) That no believer enters the fire.
D) That the Prophet will not be disgraced.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that the grave sinner, being a believer, will not be disgraced and therefore will not enter the Fire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall into a major sin, do you truly believe that Allah will not disgrace you because of your faith? How does that assurance shape your repentance?
?
Razi's proof relies on the fact that you are still a believer even after sin. Does this passage make you more hopeful or more careless about sin? Be honest—how must you respond to keep it from cheapening grace?
?
If you were standing before Allah tomorrow and He asked why you deserve not to be disgraced, would your answer rest on your deeds or on His promise in this verse? What change must you make today to live in that promise?

Preponderance of Promise Verses Over Warning Verses
Razi enumerates four proofs for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's four arguments for why verses of divine promise (وعد) are stronger and more numerous than verses of warning (وعيد) in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Promise verses outweigh threat verses by number and logic.

Razi addresses a key objection in Islamic theology: that verses promising reward seem contradicted by verses threatening punishment. He argues that the promise verses are actually preponderant for four clear reasons. This passage lays out those proofs, drawing from Qur'anic evidence and principles of Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of Razi's argumentative style.
Notice the verse citations and logical connectors.
2
Identify the four proofs
The passage enumerates four proofs with the words 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث', 'الرابع'. Locate each one and note the key evidence used.
The fourth proof is cut off; it begins with a verse from Surah an-Nisa.
3
Analyze proof one: numerical superiority
Razi's first argument is that promise verses are more numerous. He cites his own work 'al-Mahsul' to support that multiplicity of evidence gives preponderance.
4
Analyze proof two: causal implication
From verse 11:114, Razi argues that the description 'good deeds' is the cause for removing bad deeds. Therefore, every good deed should remove every bad deed, except for disbelievers.
This is based on a principle of usul al-fiqh.
5
Reflect on proofs three and four
Proof three compares the multiplication of reward (up to 700 times) to the equal recompense for evil. Proof four begins with a verse on entering paradise. Consider how all four proofs point to mercy prevailing.
The fourth proof is incomplete in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الخامس في الاعتراض: أن عمومات الوعد معارضة بعمومات الوعيد، وهي كثيرة في القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة: ٧] وبالجملة فالقرآن مملوء منها، وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد وبيان هذا الترجيح من وجوه:
الأول: أن عمومات الوعد أكثر، والأكثر راجح لأنا بينا في كتاب "المحصول في الأصول" أن الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل معتبر في الشرع.
والثاني: أن قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْحَسَنَاتِ يُذْهِبْنَ السَّيِّئَاتِ﴾ [هود: ١١٤] يدل على أن الحسنة إنما كانت مذهبة للسيئة لكونها حسنة على ما ثبت في أصول الفقه أن ترتيب الحكم على الوصف مشعر بأن علة ذلك الحكم هو ذلك الوصف، وإذا كان كذلك وجب في كل حسنة أن تكون مذهبة لكل سيئة، ترك العمل به في الحسنات الصادرة عن الكفار؛ فإنها لا تذهب سيئاتهم، وهي الكفر، فبقي معمولا به في الباقي.
الثالث: أنه تعالى قال: ﴿مَن جَاءَ بِالْحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] ثم إنه تعالى زاد على العشرة فقال: ﴿كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِّ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٍ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦١] ثم إنه زاد عليه فقال: ﴿وَاللهُ يُضَاعِفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦١] وأما في جانب السيئة فقال: ﴿وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يُجْزَى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] وهذا تنبيه ظاهر على أن جانب الحسنة راجح على جانب السيئة عند الله تعالى.
الرابع: أنه تعالى قال في آية الوعد في سورة النساء: ﴿وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَنُدْخِلُهُمْ جَنَّاتٍ تَجْرِي مِن تَحْتِهَا الْأَنْهَارُ﴾
The fifth station: The objection. That the general verses of promise are opposed by general verses of threat, and they are many in the Qur'an, such as His saying: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7). In short, the Qur'an is full of them. When you know this, we say: The general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat. The explanation of this preponderance is from aspects:
First: The general verses of promise are more numerous, and the more numerous is preponderant, because we have explained in the book 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' that preponderance by multiplicity of evidences is considered in the Sharia.
Second: His saying: 'Indeed, good deeds remove bad deeds' (11:114) indicates that a good deed removes a bad deed because it is good, as established in usul al-fiqh that attaching a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause of that ruling. If so, then every good deed must remove every bad deed. This is not applied to the good deeds of disbelievers, because they do not remove their sins—which are disbelief—so it remains operative for the rest.
Third: He said: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like thereof, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with the like thereof' (6:160). Then He increased upon ten, saying: 'Like a grain that grows seven spikes, in each spike a hundred grains' (2:261). Then He increased upon that, saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' (2:261). As for the side of evil, He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with the like thereof' (6:160). This is an apparent indication that the side of good is preponderant over the side of evil with Allah.
Fourth: He said in the verse of promise in Surah an-Nisa: 'And those who believe and do righteous deeds, We will admit them to gardens beneath which rivers flow.'
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection, arguing that the general verses promising reward outweigh those threatening punishment. Four reasons are given. First, the promise verses are more numerous, and in legal theory, multiplicity of evidence gives preponderance. Second, the verse 'good deeds remove bad deeds' implies that every good deed (except from disbelievers) removes every bad deed. Third, the Qur'an multiplies reward up to seventy times and more, while punishment is only the like, showing mercy predominates. Fourth (incomplete in the text) begins with a verse about admitting believers to gardens. The overall argument is that God's mercy outweighs His wrath, so forgiveness is more likely.

Key Terms
عمومات الوعد
عمومات الوعيد
الترجيح
كثرة الدلائل
الحسنات
السيئات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verses of threat and promise are equal; Razi argues promise verses are stronger and more numerous.
Correction: Razi demonstrates through four proofs that promise verses are preponderant due to their number, causal implication, multiplicative reward, and emphatic wording.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Razi gives for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses?
A) Promise verses are more specific.
B) Promise verses are more numerous.
C) Promise verses are easier to interpret.
D) Promise verses were revealed later.

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that the Qur'an's promise verses are preponderant over threat verses through numerical, causal, and multiplicative evidence, affirming God's mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that promise verses outweigh threat verses by number and logic. Do you actually live with the confidence that God's mercy overpowers your sins, or do you secretly doubt that your good deeds can outweigh your failures? What would you do differently today if you truly believed forgiveness is more likely than punishment?
?
The second proof uses the principle that 'good deeds remove bad deeds'—yet this is not applied to disbelievers. Are you relying on your good deeds from a time when you were neglectful of Islam? Does this lesson confront you with the need to ensure your good deeds are built on correct faith?
?
If reward is multiplied up to seven hundred times while punishment is only equal, why are you still hesitant to do even small good deeds? What is the real inhibitor in your heart—lack of trust in God's promise or attachment to the fleeting reward of this world?

Specificity of Forgiveness Verses Over General Verses
Al-Razi argues that forgiveness verses are specific and thus take precedence over general verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specific evidence (الخاص) takes precedence over general evidence (العام) in Islamic theological reasoning, as applied to verses of forgiveness.

Core Idea
Specific evidence overrides general evidence in Islamic reasoning.

In theological debates, not all Quranic verses carry the same weight—some are general in scope while others are specific. Al-Razi here makes a crucial argument: verses promising forgiveness are specific to certain circumstances and persons, whereas other verses are general. In Islamic legal and theological methodology, specific evidence takes precedence over general. This short passage establishes a key principle for prioritizing scriptural proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key terms: عفو, خاص, عام.
2
Identify the two types of evidence
The passage contrasts specific evidence (like forgiveness verses) with general evidence (like threatening verses). Identify how each is described.
3
Understand the principle of precedence
Why does the specific take precedence over the general? Reflect on the logical and methodological basis for this prioritization in Islamic thought.
This is a standard principle in usul al-fiqh and kalam.
4
Apply the argument
Consider how this argument strengthens the case for forgiveness verses being more decisive than general verses of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بها، والآيات التي تمسكنا بها في العفو لا بد أن تكون خاصة في الأحوال والأشخاص، فإنا لا نثبت العفو لكل الأشخاص، وإذا ثبت هذا لزم القطع بأن دلائلكم عامة ودلائلنا خاصة، والخاص مقدم على العام، فوجب القطع بأن الآيات الدالة على العفو مقدمة على هذه العمومات.
By it, and the verses we adhere to regarding forgiveness must be specific in circumstances and persons, for we do not establish forgiveness for all persons. And when this is established, it necessitates the certainty that your evidences are general and our evidences are specific, and the specific takes precedence over the general, so it is necessarily certain that the verses indicating forgiveness take precedence over these generalities.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument establishing that the verses of forgiveness (عفو) are specific in their application to certain circumstances and individuals, not universal. The author asserts that because these verses are specific, and the opposing available evidences are general, the specific evidence (الخاص) takes precedence over the general (العام) according to jurisprudential principle. Therefore, the forgiveness verses should be given priority over general verses of threat.

The Second Face: Generality of Verses Pertains to Disbelievers
Al-Razi argues that most general threat verses were revealed concerning disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that the general threat verses are not decisive evidence for believers, due to their context of revelation concerning disbelievers.

Core Idea
Context weakens the generality: verses about disbelievers aren't strong proof for believers.

In this passage, Imam Al-Razi presents a key argument in the debate over threat verses. Even though the principle 'the lesson is by the generality of the wording' is accepted, he shows that when the revelation context is specific to disbelievers, the verse's application to others is only probable, not certain. This subtle point affects how we use these verses in theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the logical flow: statement, concession, qualification.
Pay attention to the phrase 'على ما اشتمل على شرحها كتب التفسير'.
2
Identify the two principles
The author states the principle 'العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب' but then qualifies it. Understand both the rule and its limitation.
3
Analyze the strength of indication
Notice the terms 'قوية قاطعة' vs 'ظنية ضعيفة'. Al-Razi argues that the indication outside the cause is weak. Why is that important?
This relates to the decisive nature of the theological issue.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that these verses cannot be used as decisive evidence. Think about how this shapes Al-Razi's overall argument.
Contrast with those who argue for absolute generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : هو أن أكثر هذه العمومات نازلة في حق الكفار، على ما اشتمل على شرحها كتب التفسير، ونحن و إن كنا نعتقد بأن العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب إلا أنا نعتقد أن دلالته في غير محل السبب ليست دلالة قوية قاطعة، بل دلالة ظنية ضعيفة، وإذا كان كذلك لم تكن دلالتها على الاستغراق قوية صالحة لأن يتمسك بها في هذه المسألة القطعية.
The second aspect: that most of these general verses were revealed concerning the disbelievers, as the books of exegesis contain their explanation. And even though we believe that the lesson is by the generality of the wording, not by the specificity of the cause, yet we believe that its indication in other than the place of the cause is not a strong, decisive indication, but rather a weak, probable indication. And if that is the case, then their indication of inclusiveness is not strong enough to be used as evidence in this decisive matter.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (الوجه الثاني) concerning the general verses of threat. Al-Razi points out that most of these verses were revealed about disbelievers, as explained in tafsir works. Although the principle in usul al-fiqh is that the lesson is taken from the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the occasion of revelation, the author argues that when a verse is revealed about a specific situation (like disbelievers), its application beyond that situation is not decisive (قطعي) but only probable (ظني). Thus, these verses cannot be used as decisive evidence in such a definitive matter.

Key Terms
عمومات
نازلة
كفار
عبرة
عموم اللفظ
خصوص السبب
دلالة ظنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General verses in the Quran apply with full strength to all people regardless of the context of revelation.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that when a verse is revealed about disbelievers, its indication for others is only probable, not certain, so it cannot be used as decisive evidence in definitive matters.

Checkpoint

A) Because they are not in the Quran
B) Because they were revealed about disbelievers, so their incative strength is reduced
C) Because they are ambiguous
D) Because they contradict other verses

Key Takeaway
The context of revelation reduces the strength of a general verse's indication, making it unsuitable as conclusive proof in decisive theological issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Objection and First Reason: Preponderance of Promise Verses by Quantity
Al-Razi presents the objection of conflicting promise and threat verses and gives the first reason for preponderance: greater quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first argument for the preponderance of promise verses over threat verses: the quantitative reasoning that promise verses are more numerous, and why this is considered valid in Sharia.

Core Idea
Quantity of scriptural evidence can tip the balance in theological arguments.

In this passage, Al-Razi presents a key objection in the debate between proponents of promise and threat. He acknowledges the existence of numerous threat verses but claims that promise verses are more abundant. He then invokes a principle from usul al-fiqh to argue that the greater quantity of evidences gives preponderance. This lesson unpacks that first reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that Al-Razi is addressing the objection that promise verses are opposed by threat verses. He then gives the first reason for preponderance.
The passage mentions 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' – a work on usul al-fiqh.
2
Distinguish between promise and threat verses
Identify the two types: 'عمومات الوعد' (general promise verses) and 'عمومات الوعيد' (general threat verses). Understand that they appear to conflict, and Al-Razi seeks to show that promise outweighs threat.
Example of promise verse: Quran 99:7 about seeing good deeds.
3
Grasp the quantitative argument
Al-Razi claims that promise verses are more numerous. He then asserts that the more numerous evidences are preponderant. This is based on the principle established in his usul al-fiqh work.
The phrase 'كثرة الدلائل' (abundance of evidences) is key.
4
Consider the principle's validity
Al-Razi says this principle is 'معتبر في الشرع' (considered in Sharia). Reflect on why quantity might be a valid basis for preference in scriptural interpretation.
This is a methodological point from usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الخامس في الاعتراض: أن عمومات الوعد معارضة بعمومات الوعيد، وهي كثيرة في القرآن كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَن يَعْمَلْ مِثْقَالَ ذَرَّةٍ خَيْرًا يَرَهُ﴾ [الزلزلة : ٧] وبالجملة فالقرآن مملوء منها، و إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد وبيان هذا الترجيح من وجوه : الأول : أن عمومات الوعد أكثر ، والأكثر راجح لأنا بينا في كتاب" المحصول في الأصول " أن الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل معتبر في الشرع.
The fifth station regarding the objection: that the general verses of promise are opposed by the general verses of threat, and they are many in the Quran, such as His statement: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' [99:7]. In summary, the Quran is filled with them. And when you know this, we say: the general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat, and the explanation of this preponderance is from several aspects: The first: that the general verses of promise are more numerous, and the more numerous is preponderant, because we have explained in the book 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul' that preponderance by the abundance of evidences is considered in the Sharia.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage begins with the fifth station (المقام الخامس) which is the objection that the general promise verses are opposed by general threat verses. Al-Razi then claims that the promise verses are preponderant (راجحة) over the threat verses. He then gives the first of several proofs: the promise verses are more numerous in the Quran. The principle of preferring the more abundant evidence is established in his work 'al-Mahsul fi al-Usul', which is a work on usul al-fiqh. This quantitative argument is the first reason.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that the principle of preponderance by abundance of evidences is considered valid in Sharia, as established in his usul al-fiqh work.

B) They are more numerous
C) They are from the Quran

Good Deeds Remove Bad Deeds
Al-Razi cites verse 11:114 and applies a usul principle to argue for the removal of sins by good deeds, with an exception for disbelievers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the verse 'Good deeds remove bad deeds' and the usul al-fiqh principle to argue that every good deed removes corresponding sins, with the exception of disbelievers.

Core Idea
Good deeds erase sins, but not for disbelievers.

Al-Razi presents his second proof for the preponderance of promise over threat by citing a Quranic verse and applying a logical principle from usul al-fiqh. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and the important exception for disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the translation. Notice how Al-Razi quotes the verse and then uses a principle to derive a general rule.
2
Understand the usul principle
Focus on the phrase 'attachment of a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause.' This is key to Al-Razi's argument.
This principle is foundational in Islamic legal theory.
3
Note the exception
Al-Razi explicitly excludes disbelievers from the rule because their major sin (disbelief) is not removed by good deeds. Think about why this matters for the overall argument about promise and threat.
This exception preserves the threat of punishment for disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : : أن قوله تعالى : وإن الحسنات يذهبن السيئات ﴾ [هود: ١١٤] يدل على أن الحسنة إنما كانت مذهبة للسيئة لكونها حسنة على ما ثبت في أصول الفقه أن ترتيب الحكم على الوصف مشعر بأن علة ذلك الحكم هو ذلك الوصف، و إذا كان كذلك وجب في كل حسنة أن تكون مذهبة لكل سيئة ترك العمل به في الحسنات الصادرة عن الكفار؛ فإنها لا تذهب سيئاتهم، وهي الكفر ، فبقي معمولا بها في الباقي.
And the second: that His statement 'And indeed good deeds remove bad deeds' (Hud 114) indicates that the good deed is removing the bad deed because it is good, according to what is established in usul al-fiqh that the attachment of a ruling to a description suggests that the description is the cause of that ruling. And if that is the case, then it is necessary for every good deed to be removing every bad deed, except that we do not apply it to the good deeds emanating from disbelievers, for they do not remove their bad deeds, which are disbelief, so it remains operative in the rest.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the second proof. Al-Razi cites the verse 'Good deeds remove bad deeds' and applies the usul al-fiqh principle that when a ruling is attached to a description, that description is the cause ('illa). Here, the good deed is the cause of removing the bad deed. Hence, every good deed should remove every bad deed, except that this is not applied to disbelievers because their good deeds do not remove the major sin of disbelief. Therefore, the rule applies to believers, showing that promise (good deeds being rewarded) is preponderant.

Key Terms
حسنات
سيئات
مذهبة
وصف
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that any good deed automatically removes all sins for anyone, including disbelievers.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that this rule does not apply to disbelievers because their major sin (disbelief) is not removed by their good deeds.

Checkpoint
Why do good deeds of disbelievers not remove their bad deeds according to this passage?
A) Because they are not sincere
B) Because their bad deed (disbelief) is greater
C) Because the rule does not apply to them
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
Good deeds remove bad deeds for believers, but not for disbelievers, because their disbelief remains unforgiven.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever committed a sin thinking that your other good deeds will automatically erase it? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what will you do today to repent sincerely instead of relying on future good deeds?
?
The exception for disbelievers shows that without faith, even good deeds cannot remove the greatest sin of disbelief. Are you actively concerned about the state of your own faith, or do you assume your good deeds suffice? What is one area where you need to strengthen your faith so that your deeds are accepted?
?
If you are a believer, your good deeds remove bad deeds—but this does not give license to sin. Is there a sin you have been tolerating, secretly hoping your other good deeds will cover it? Name it now before God, and commit to a specific action to stop it.

The Multiplication of Good Deeds: Evidence of Mercy
Al-Razi uses the tenfold reward for good and exact recompense for evil to show preponderance of promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the Quranic contrast between multiplied reward for good deeds and exact recompense for evil deeds to argue for the preponderance of God's promise over His threat.

Core Idea
God's mercy outweighs His justice: good deeds are multiplied, evil deeds are recompensed equally.

Al-Razi presents the third argument for the preponderance of promise. He cites clear Quranic verses that show God's treatment of good and evil is not symmetrical: good deeds are rewarded many times over, while evil deeds receive only their due. This contrast, he argues, is an apparent indication that God's mercy prevails.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the verses cited by Al-Razi: al-An'am 160 and al-Baqarah 261. Notice the exact wording about good and evil deeds.
The verses are the core evidence for this argument.
2
Observe the escalation for good deeds
Al-Razi highlights a three-stage escalation: tenfold, then a grain producing seven hundred, then unlimited multiplication by God's will.
This escalation shows God's immense generosity.
3
Contrast with evil deeds
For evil deeds, there is no escalation: only a like-for-like recompense. This contrast clearly indicates the preponderance of the side of good.
The contrast itself is the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه تعالى قال : المَن جَاءَ بِالحَسَنَةِ فَلَهُ عَشْرُ أَمْثَالِهَا وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَيْئَةِ فَلَا يجزى إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦] ثم إنه تعالى زاد على العشرة فقال : كَمَثَلِ حَبَّةٍ أَنْبَتَتْ سَبْعَ سَنَابِلَ فِي كُلِ سُنْبُلَةٍ مِائَةُ حَبَّةٌ [البَقَرَة : ٢٦١] ثم إنه زاد عليه فقال : والله يضعفُ لِمَن يَشَاءُ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ٢٦١] وأما في جانب السيئة فقال: ﴿وَمَن جَاءَ بِالسَّيِّئَةِ فَلَا يجزء إِلَّا مِثْلَهَا ﴾ [الأنعام: ١٦٠] وهذا تنبيه ظاهر على أن جانب الحسنة راجح على جانب السيئة عند الله تعالى.
The third: that He, exalted, said: 'Whoever comes with a good deed will have ten times the like of it, and whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with its like' (al-An'am 160). Then He, exalted, increased upon the ten, saying: 'Like a grain that grows seven ears, in every ear a hundred grains' (al-Baqarah 261). Then He increased upon that, saying: 'And Allah multiplies for whom He wills' (al-Baqarah 261). And as for the side of evil, He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be recompensed except with its like' (al-An'am 160). And this is an apparent indication that the side of good deeds is preponderant over the side of evil deeds with Allah, exalted.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the third proof. Al-Razi cites verses showing that God multiplies the reward for good deeds up to many times (starting from ten to seven hundred to even more by His will), while for evil deeds, recompense is only equal to the deed (like for like). This contrast clearly indicates that the side of good deeds (حسنات) is given more weight by God, demonstrating the preponderance of promise over threat.

Key Terms
حسنة
سيئة
عشر أمثال
مضاعفة
مثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the multiplication only applies to specific cases and not as a general principle.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that the progression from ten to seven hundred to unlimited shows a general principle of God's mercy and preponderance of promise.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does God's treatment of good deeds compare to evil deeds?
A) Both are multiplied
B) Good deeds are multiplied, evil deeds are recompensed equally
C) Evil deeds are multiplied more
D) Both are recompensed equally

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi demonstrates that the Quranic contrast between multiplied reward for good and equal recompense for evil is a clear indication that God's mercy outweighs His justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that Allah will only recompense you with its like, or do you fear He will magnify your punishment? How does this lesson reshape your hope in His mercy?
?
Allah multiplies good deeds without limit for whom He wills. What is one good deed you have been neglecting, and what is stopping you from doing it right now, knowing that its reward could be multiplied beyond counting?
?
If you were to calculate your good deeds versus your evil deeds today, would you feel confident that Allah's side of mercy outweighs your faults? If not, what specific step will you take this week to tip the scale?

5 min
Emphasis on Promise Not on Threat
Al-Razi contrasts emphatic promise verses with unemphatic threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the Qur'an emphasizes promise (wa'd) but not threat (wa'id), and why this supports the priority of divine mercy.

Core Idea
God emphasizes promise but never threat.

Al-Razi presents a subtle linguistic observation: the Qur'an uses emphatic phrases like 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' for promises, but never says 'Wa'id Allah Haqqa'. This asymmetry reveals God's greater care for rewarding believers than for punishing sinners. In this lesson, we will examine the verses he cites and unpack the reasoning behind this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without translation. Notice the contrast between the promise verse (4:122) and the threat-related verse (Qaf 29).
The OCR for the Qaf verse may have errors; check your own mushaf if needed.
2
Identify the key contrast
Find where Al-Razi points out that 'the promise of Allah, true' (wa'd Allah haqqa) is used, but 'the threat of Allah, true' is never used.
This is the core observation.
3
Understand the Qaf verse
Al-Razi mentions Qaf 29, which says the word is not changed. He argues this applies to disbelievers and includes both promise and threat, but does not contain the specific emphasis on threat.
The verse is not a counterexample; it is neutral.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why God emphasizes promise but not threat. What does this tell us about God's attributes and His treatment of believers versus disbelievers?
This connects to the broader theme of mercy prevailing over wrath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : أنه تعالى قال في آية الوعد في سورة النساء ﴿وَالَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ سَنُدْخِلُهُمْ جَنَّاتٍ تَجْرِى مِن تحتها الأنهار خالدين فيها أبدا وَعْدَ اللَّهِ حَقًّا وَمَنْ أَصْدَقُ منَ اللهِ قِيلًا ﴾ [النساء : ۱۲۲] فقوله: ﴿وَعْدَ اللَّهِ حَقًّا ﴾ [النساء : ١٢٢] إنما ذكره للتأكيد، ولم يقل في شيء من القرآن وعيد الله حقا. وأما قوله تعالى : لا مَا يُبدل القولُ لَدَى وَمَا أَنَا بِظَلَمِ الْعِيدِ [ق: ۲۹] فهو بإجماع المفسرين في حق الكفار، وأيضا قوله: ﴿مَا يُبَدَّلُ الْقَوْلُ لَدَى ﴾ [ق: ٢٩] يتناول الوعد والوعيد.
The fourth: that He, exalted, said in the verse of promise in Surah al-Nisa: 'And those who believe and do righteous deeds, We will admit them into gardens beneath which rivers flow, abiding therein forever. It is the promise of Allah, true, and who is more truthful in speech than Allah?' (4:122). So His saying 'the promise of Allah, true' — He mentioned it for emphasis, and He did not say in any part of the Quran 'the threat of Allah, true'. And as for His statement: 'The word is not changed with me, and I am not unjust to the servants' (Qaf 29) — it is, by consensus of the exegetes, concerning the disbelievers. Also, His saying 'the word is not changed with me' includes both promise and threat.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This is the fourth proof. Al-Razi points out that in the promise verse (4:122), God emphasizes the promise with the phrase 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' (the promise of Allah is true) and then asks 'who is more truthful than Allah?' This emphasis is not found in any threat verse; nowhere in the Quran does it say 'the threat of Allah is true'. As for the verse in Qaf 29, it is about disbelievers and the word 'القول' includes both promise and threat, but the specific emphasis on promise is unique. This shows that God's care for promise is greater.

Misconception: Some might think that threat verses are equally emphasized in the Quran.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that nowhere does the Quran say 'the threat of Allah is true' with the same explicit emphasis as 'the promise of Allah is true'.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an emphasizes promise with 'Wa'd Allah Haqqa' but never uses such emphasis for threat, indicating God's mercy outweighs His wrath.

Fifth Reason: Seeking Forgiveness Guarantees Pardon
Al-Razi uses verses showing that seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness, without threat counterpart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses Quranic verses to argue that seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness, demonstrating the preponderance of mercy.

Core Idea
Seeking forgiveness without repentance still brings Allah's mercy.

Al-Razi presents a fifth proof for the predominance of mercy: the Qur'an promises forgiveness to those who seek forgiveness, without conditioning it on repentance. This asymmetry—promise of forgiveness for sin but no threat of punishment—shows that Allah emphasizes mercy over wrath. The passage cites two verses from Surah al-Nisa and one from al-Isra.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to understand the flow of Al-Razi's argument. Note the verse citations.
The passage contrasts the treatment of good deeds and sins.
2
Distinguish istighfar from tawbah
Notice that Al-Razi defines istighfar (seeking forgiveness) and says it is not the same as tawbah (repentance). This distinction is crucial for his argument.
3
Analyze the asymmetry
Observe that in the first verse, forgiveness is promised. In the second, no punishment is threatened. Then Al-Razi cites al-Isra 7 where good deeds are mentioned twice, evil once. Reflect on how this shows mercy's dominance.
This asymmetry is the core evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَن يَعْمَلْ سُوءًا أَوْ يَظْلِمُ نَفْسَهُ ثُم يستغفر الله يجد الله غَفُورًا رَّحِيمًا﴾ [النساء : ۱۱۰] ﴿وَمَن يَكْسِبْ إِثْمًا فَإنَّما يكسبه على نَفْسِهِ وَكَانَ الله عليما حكيما﴾ [النساء : ۱۱۱] والاستغفار طلب المغفرة، وطلب المغفرة ليس نفس التوبة. وخرج عن هذه الآية بأنه سواء تاب أو لم يتب فإذا استغفر الله غفر الله له ، ولم يقل في جانب المعصية : "ومن يكسب إنما " فإنه يجد الله معذبًا معاقبا ، بل قال : ﴿فَإنَّمَا يَكْسِبُهُ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ ﴾ [النساء : ١١١] فدلت هذه الآيات على أن جانب الحسنة راجح عند الله، ونظيره قوله تعالى: ﴿إِن أَحْسَنتُمْ أَحْسَنتُمْ لأَنفُسِكُمْ وَإِنْ أَسَأْتُمْ فَلَهَا﴾ [الإسراء: ۷] ولم يقل : " وإن أسأتم لها فكأنه تعالى بالغ في إظهار أفعاله الحسنة، حيث ذكرها مرتين وستر عليه إساءته حيث لم يذكرها إلا مرة واحدة، وكل ذلك يدل على أن جانب الحسنات راجح.
The fifth: His statement: 'And whoever does evil or wrongs himself then seeks forgiveness of Allah will find Allah forgiving and merciful' (4:110), 'And whoever commits a sin only commits it against himself, and Allah is Knowing and Wise' (4:111). And seeking forgiveness (istighfar) is asking for forgiveness, and asking for forgiveness is not the same as repentance (tawbah). And from this verse it is understood that whether he repents or does not repent, when he asks forgiveness of Allah, Allah forgives him. And He did not say regarding the side of sin: 'Whoever commits a sin, he will find Allah punishing and afflicting', but rather said: 'He only commits it against himself.' So these verses indicate that the side of good deeds is preponderant with Allah. And similar is His statement: 'If you do good, you do good to yourselves; and if you do evil, it is to yourselves' (17:7) — and He did not say 'you do evil to yourselves'. It is as if He, exalted, emphasized His good deeds by mentioning them twice, and concealed the evil deed by mentioning it only once, and all that indicates that the side of good deeds is preponderant.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is the fifth proof. Al-Razi cites two verses from Surah al-Nisa. The first promises forgiveness for those who seek forgiveness after doing evil. The second states that sin only harms the sinner. The author distinguishes istighfar (seeking forgiveness) from tawbah (repentance) and argues that even without repentance, seeking forgiveness leads to forgiveness. Importantly, God does not promise punishment for sin in the second verse, but merely says the sin is against the person. This asymmetry shows that the side of good deeds (promise) is given priority. Al-Razi also cites al-Isra 7, where God emphasizes good deeds by mentioning them twice and mentions evil only once, further indicating mercy's predominance.

Key Terms
استغفار
توبة
مغفرة
حسنة
سيئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeking forgiveness without repentance is futile; true forgiveness requires repentance first.
Correction: Al-Razi explicitly states that whether one repents or not, if he seeks forgiveness, Allah forgives him based on the verse. Istighfar itself can bring forgiveness.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the effect of seeking forgiveness (استغفار) even without repentance?
A) It is rejected
B) Allah forgives
C) It counts only if repentance follows
D) It has no effect

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic promise of forgiveness for those who seek it, even without repentance, shows that Allah's mercy outweighs His wrath.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do something wrong, do you rush to say 'Astaghfirullah' even before you feel ready to stop the sin completely? Or do you hold back, thinking your istighfar is worthless without a perfect repentance? What would you need to change in your heart to believe that your simple plea can be accepted?

This lesson shows that Allah mentions good deeds twice and evil only once. Yet in your own mind, do you often replay your sins more than your good deeds? How can you train yourself to magnify Allah's mercy over your failures, starting today?
?
