Sixth Reason: Repetition and Emphasis on Forgiveness
Al-Razi argues that the repetition of the forgiveness verse indicates God's greater care for promise over threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the repetition of Qur'anic verses to argue that God emphasizes His promise of forgiveness over His threats.

Core Idea
God's repetition of the forgiveness verse shows His care for promise outweighs His diligence in threat.

In this lesson, Al-Razi presents his sixth proof for the preponderance of God's mercy. He notes that the verse on forgiving minor sins is repeated twice in the same chapter, while no threat verse is repeated in such a manner. This repetition indicates God's greater care for His promise of forgiveness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic and English versions to understand the argument's flow.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Razi claims that the repetition of the forgiveness verse shows God's care for promise is more complete than His diligence in threat.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does repetition imply emphasis? Why is the lack of repetition for threats significant? This is the core of the proof.
Consider that repetition in the Qur'an is often for emphasis.
4
Connect to broader theme
This argument supports the view that God's mercy prevails over His wrath, a key theme in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أنا سنبين إن شاء الله تعالى أن قوله: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يَغْفِرُ أَن يُشْرَكَ بِهِ وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاء﴾ [النساء : ٤٨] لا يدل إلا على العفو عن صاحب الكبيرة، ثم إنه تعالى أعاد هذه الآية في السورة الواحدة مرتين والإعادة لا تحسن إلا للتأكيد، ولم يذكر شيئًا آیات من الوعيد على وجه الإعادة بلفظ واحد لا في سورة واحدة ولا في سور كثيرة، فدل على أن عناية الله في الوعد أتم من مبالغته في جانب الوعيد.
The sixth: we will explain, God willing, that His statement: 'Indeed Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48) indicates only the forgiveness of the sinner of a major sin (kabira). Then He, exalted, repeated this verse in the same surah twice, and repetition is only good for emphasis. And He did not mention any verses of threat in the way of repetition with a single wording, neither in one surah nor in many surahs. This indicates that God's care for promise is more complete than His diligence regarding the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This is the sixth proof. Al-Razi refers to the famous verse 'Indeed Allah does not forgive association with Him, and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48). He notes that this verse is repeated twice in the same surah (Surah al-Nisa). Repetition is a form of emphasis. No threat verse is repeated in such a manner. This shows that God's care (عناية) for promise is greater than His diligence (مبالغة) in threat, further proving the preponderance of promise verses.

Key Terms

عفو
تأكيد
إعادة
عناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Repetition in the Qur'an is meaningless or just for poetic effect.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that repetition serves the purpose of emphasis, especially when it comes to God's promise of forgiveness, showing His greater care.

Checkpoint
What does the repetition of the forgiveness verse (4:48) indicate according to Al-Razi?
A) That forgiveness is conditional
B) That God emphasizes promise over threat
C) That the verse is not important
D) That threat is also repeated

Key Takeaway
God's repetition of the forgiveness verse twice emphasizes His promise of forgiveness over His warnings.

Seventh Reason: Interpreting Threat Verses Is Better
Al-Razi explains the seventh reason: custom supports interpreting threat verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that when promise and threat verses conflict, it is better to interpret (ta'wil) the threat verses because forgiveness of a threat is socially admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved.

Core Idea
Custom favors forgiveness of threats but condemns neglecting promises.

Al-Razi's seventh reason is a methodological argument based on social custom ('urf). He argues that when general verses of promise and threat conflict, we must interpret one side. Since forgiving a threat is praiseworthy but ignoring a promise is blameworthy, we should interpret threat verses in a way that preserves the promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the seventh reason
Read the Arabic text for the seventh reason along with its literal translation. Notice the flow from conflict to necessity of interpretation to the preference for interpreting threats.
The passage states that forgiveness from threat is 'mustahsan' (considered good) in custom.
2
Understand the custom argument
Al-Razi uses social norm: forgiving a threat is good, neglecting a promise is bad. Why does this matter? Because interpreting a threat away (making it non-literal) allows forgiveness, whereas interpreting a promise away would mean neglecting it, which is socially reprehensible.
3
Apply the reasoning
Therefore, when choosing which set of verses to interpret allegorically, the side of threat should be chosen. This preserves the literal meaning of promise verses and harmonizes the conflicting categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن عمومات الوعيد والوعد لما تعارضت فلا بد من تأويل أحد الجانبين، وصرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أحسن من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد؛ وذلك لأن العفو عن الوعيد مستحسن في العرف، أما إهمال الو عد فإنه مستقبح، فكان صرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أولى من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد. الثامن: أن القرآن مملوء من كونه غافرًا وغفوراً، وأن له الغفران والمغفرة، وأنه رحيم كريم، وله العفو والإحسان، والفضل والإفضال، والأخبار الدالة على هذه المعاني قد بلغت مبلغًا عظيمًا في الكثرة والقوة، وليس في القرآن ما يدل على أنه بعيد عن الصفح والتجاوز والكرم، وكل ذلك يفيد رجحان جانب الوعد على جانب الوعيد.
The seventh: that since the general verses of threat and promise are conflicting, it is necessary to interpret one of the two sides. And directing the interpretation to the side of threat is better than directing it to the side of promise. That is because forgiveness from threat is considered good in custom, whereas neglecting promise is considered bad. So directing interpretation to the side of threat is more appropriate than directing it to the side of promise. The eighth: that the Quran is filled with descriptions of Him as Forgiving (Ghafir, Ghafur), and that He has forgiveness and pardon, and that He is Merciful, Generous, and He has pardon and benevolence, and grace and favor. And the reports indicating these meanings have reached a great level in abundance and strength. And there is nothing in the Quran that indicates that He is far from pardon, overlooking, and generosity. And all of that benefits the preponderance of the side of promise over the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This lesson combines the seventh and eighth reasons. The seventh reason argues that when the general verses of promise and threat conflict, we should apply interpretation (ta'wil) to the threat verses rather than the promise verses. This is because in social custom ('urf), forgiveness from a threat is admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved. Therefore, it is more fitting to interpret threat verses in a way that avoids their literal meaning (so that forgiveness can still apply) rather than to interpret promise verses away. The eighth reason observes that the Quran is replete with attributes of forgiveness and mercy, and there is no verse suggesting God is unyielding. This abundance of mercy-related attributes further confirms the preponderance of promise over threat.

Key Terms
تعارض
تأويل
عفو
إهمال الوعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all verses must be taken literally without any allegorical interpretation.
Correction: Al-Razi acknowledges that conflict requires interpretation. He provides a rational criterion for which side to interpret, based on social norms of forgiveness and promise-keeping.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's seventh reason, why is it better to interpret threat verses rather than promise verses?
A) Because threat verses are more numerous
B) Because forgiveness from threat is socially admired, while neglecting a promise is bad
C) Because threat verses are unclear
D) Because promise verses are stronger

Key Takeaway
Customary norms of forgiveness and promise-keeping guide the interpretation of conflicting scriptural verses, favoring the side of promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone threatens you, do you instinctively seek to enforce the threat or to forgive? According to this lesson, custom favors forgiveness. Which path have you followed recently, and what does that say about your character?
?
Think of a promise you made to Allah or to others that you have neglected. How does this lesson's emphasis on the blameworthiness of neglecting promises convict you? Will you take a concrete step to fulfill it today?
?
Consider the balance of mercy and justice in your own dealings: Are you quicker to demand justice for others' faults (like threats) but lenient on your own promises? How does this principle from Al-Razi call you to realign your priorities?

Eighth Reason: Abundance of Mercy Verses
Al-Razi argues the abundance of mercy verses shows promise's preponderance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's argument that the Quran's overwhelming focus on divine mercy and forgiveness indicates the preponderance of promise over threat.

Core Idea
The Quran is filled with mercy, none with harshness.

Al-Razi's eighth reason is a textual argument: the Quran abounds with verses describing God as Forgiving, Merciful, Generous, and Benevolent. These attributes appear in great number and strength, while no verse suggests that God is far from pardon. This abundance weighs the scale in favor of promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the eighth reason
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the list of divine attributes and the statement about their abundance and strength.
The passage ends with the conclusion that all of this benefits the preponderance of promise over threat.
2
Identify the key attributes mentioned
Al-Razi enumerates: Ghafir, Ghafur, forgiveness, mercy, generosity, pardon, benevolence, grace, favor. Each emphasizes divine mercy.
3
Understand the argument from abundance
The sheer frequency and strength of mercy-related verses, combined with the absence of verses indicating harshness, tip the balance in favor of promise. This is a cumulative evidence argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : أن عمومات الوعيد والوعد لما تعارضت فلا بد من تأويل أحد الجانبين، وصرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أحسن من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد؛ وذلك لأن العفو عن الوعيد مستحسن في العرف، أما إهمال الو عد فإنه مستقبح، فكان صرف التأويل إلى جانب الوعيد أولى من صرفه إلى جانب الوعد. الثامن: أن القرآن مملوء من كونه غافرًا وغفوراً، وأن له الغفران والمغفرة، وأنه رحيم كريم، وله العفو والإحسان، والفضل والإفضال، والأخبار الدالة على هذه المعاني قد بلغت مبلغًا عظيمًا في الكثرة والقوة، وليس في القرآن ما يدل على أنه بعيد عن الصفح والتجاوز والكرم، وكل ذلك يفيد رجحان جانب الوعد على جانب الوعيد.
The seventh: that since the general verses of threat and promise are conflicting, it is necessary to interpret one of the two sides. And directing the interpretation to the side of threat is better than directing it to the side of promise. That is because forgiveness from threat is considered good in custom, whereas neglecting promise is considered bad. So directing interpretation to the side of threat is more appropriate than directing it to the side of promise. And the reports indicating these meanings have reached a great level in abundance and strength. And there is nothing in the Quran that indicates that He is far from pardon, overlooking, and generosity. And all of that benefits the preponderance of the side of promise over the side of threat.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This lesson combines the seventh and eighth reasons. The seventh reason argues that when the general verses of promise and threat conflict, we should apply interpretation (ta'wil) to the threat verses rather than the promise verses. This is because in social custom ('urf), forgiveness from a threat is admired, while neglecting a promise is disapproved. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You claim to believe that promise outweighs threat, but does your daily prayer reflect that? Do you ask for forgiveness with the same confidence as you ask for provision? If not, what needs to change in your understanding of who God is?

The Tenth Proof: Faith Outweighs Sin
Imam Razi quotes Yahya ibn Mu'adh's argument for the precedence of promise using the logic of faith outweighing sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument from Yahya ibn Mu'adh that faith outweighs sin, and how it supports the doctrine of the precedence of divine promise over threat.

Core Idea
If a moment of faith can erase decades of disbelief, it can also erase a moment of sin.

Imam Razi presents the tenth proof, quoting the early ascetic Yahya ibn Mu'adh. This argument uses a simple comparison to show that if the greatest evil (disbelief) can be erased by a small act of faith, then surely lesser sins are also overshadowed by faith. Razi endorses this reasoning as 'good speech.' Let's examine the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to get the overall sense.
Notice that the passage begins with the number 'tenth' and then directly quotes Yahya ibn Mu'adh.
2
Identify the key analogy
Yahya compares the power of tawhid over disbelief to its power over sin. If a single hour of faith can demolish fifty years of disbelief, shouldn't it also demolish a single hour of sin?
The comparison hinges on the relative severity: disbelief is worse than sin.
3
Understand the justice argument
The passage then argues from justice: since disbelief does not benefit (it is pure evil), justice requires that faith (pure good) not be harmed by any sins. Otherwise, disbelief would be considered greater than faith.
This is a logical implication: if a lesser evil could nullify a greater good, then the greater good would be less powerful, which contradicts the principle of justice.
4
Note the conclusion: hope of pardon
Even if one rejects the logical argument, there remains at least hope of pardon (rajā' al-'afw). Imam Razi calls this 'good speech.'
This shows that the argument is meant to instill hope, not complacency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : قال يحيى بن معاذ : إلهي إن كان توحيد ساعة يهدم كفر خمسين سنة، كيف لا يهدم معصية ساعة ؟ إلهي لما كان الكفر لا ينفع مقتضى العدل أن الإيمان لا يضر معه شيء من المعاصين وإلا فالكفر أعظم من الإيمان، فإن لم يكن كذلك فلا أقل من رجاء العفو، وهذا كلام حسن.
The tenth: Yahya ibn Mu'adh said: My God, if the tawhid of an hour demolishes the disbelief of fifty years, how does it not demolish the disobedience of an hour? My God, since disbelief does not benefit, the requirement of justice is that faith is not harmed along with it by any of the disobediences. Otherwise, disbelief would be greater than faith. If that is not the case, then at least there is hope of pardon. And this is good speech.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof, which is a quotation from the early ascetic Yahya ibn Mu'adh. The argument is based on a comparison between the weight of faith and disbelief. If a single hour of tawhid (monotheism) can erase fifty years of disbelief (kufr), then surely it should also erase the sin of one hour, which is far less severe. The reasoning continues: since disbelief, which is the greatest evil, does not benefit the one who holds it, justice (al-'adl) implies that faith, being the greatest good, should not be nullified by any sins. If sins could nullify faith, then disbelief would be considered greater than faith (since it can only be erased by faith itself). If this logic is not accepted, then at least there is hope for pardon (rajā' al-'afw) for the sinner. Imam Razi endorses this as 'good speech' (kalam hasan).

Key Terms
يحيى بن معاذ
توحيد
كفر
معصية
العدل
رجاء العفو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument implies that sins are harmless and repentance is unnecessary.
Correction: Yahya ibn Mu'adh is arguing from the principle of justice, not license. The argument is meant to show the overwhelming mercy of Allah and the power of faith, not to encourage sin. Imam Razi includes it as a proof for the precedence of promise over threat, not as a justification for sinful behavior.

Checkpoint

A) It cannot be erased
B) It can also be erased by that same tawhid
C) It is greater than disbelief
D) It requires repentance

Key Takeaway
Yahya ibn Mu'adh's argument shows that if faith can overcome disbelief, it can certainly overcome sin, leading to hope of pardon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single hour of sincere tawhid can outweigh fifty years of disbelief, what sin are you holding onto that you have not yet brought to God in repentance?
?
This argument ends with 'hope of pardon'. Is your hope in God's pardon a genuine hope that moves you to action, or is it a cheap cover for complacency?
?
Yahya ibn Mu'adh argues from God's justice. How does your understanding of God's justice affect the way you treat your own sins and the sins of others?

The Principle of Specific vs. General Verses
Imam Razi argues for preponderance of promise verses using specificity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's thirteenth proof, which applies the principle that specific verses (e.g., of forgiveness) take precedence over general verses (e.g., of threat) to argue for the preponderance of hope over fear.

Core Idea
The specific overrides the general in theological interpretation.

Imam Razi presents his final proof in this section, a refined argument from legal theory. Using the established principle that specific texts outweigh general ones when they conflict, he argues that the verses promising forgiveness are necessarily specific, while the verses threatening punishment are general. This gives them interpretive weight, undermining the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall sense, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow from conflict to preponderance.
This passage is short but dense; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the conflict
Imam Razi begins by stating that general verses of promise and threat are in conflict. Understand that 'promise' refers to verses about reward and 'threat' to verses about punishment.
3
Understand the why of preponderance
He gives preponderance to verses of promise using verses of pardon. Note his reasoning: pardon verses must be specific, threat verses must be general. Why? Because if threat verses were also specific, they would not serve their deterrent purpose.
This is a key move in the argument.
4
Apply the principle of specificity
He then invokes the principle that when a specific text and a general text conflict, the specific takes precedence. Conclude that promise verses are preponderant.
This principle comes from usul al-fiqh and is applied here to theology.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Imam Razi states that with these proofs, the Mu'tazila's arguments are completely refuted. Consider why this refutation is considered complete.
The Mu'tazila emphasized threat; this argument diminishes its weight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث عشر : أن عمومات الوعد والوعيد لما تعارضت فنحن نرجح عمومات الوعد بالآيات الدالة على العفو والغفران وهذا ترجيح يجب المصير إليه، وذلك لأن آيات العفو لا بد أن تكون خاصة وآيات العقوبة والوعيد لا بد أن تكون عامة، وإلا لم تدل على مقصودهم، والعام والخاص إذا تعارضا ترجح الخاص على العام. فثبت بمجموع هذه الوجوه أن عمومات الوعد راجحة على عمومات الوعيد، فسقط كلام المعتزلة بالكلية.
The thirteenth: That the general verses of promise and threat, when they conflict, we give preponderance to the general verses of promise by the verses indicating pardon and forgiveness, and this is a preponderance that must be adopted. And that is because the verses of pardon must be specific and the verses of punishment and threat must be general; otherwise they would not indicate their intended meaning. And the general and the specific, when they conflict, the specific is given preponderance over the general. So it is established by the totality of these proofs that the general verses of promise are preponderant over the general verses of threat, and the speech of the Mu'tazila falls completely.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the thirteenth and final proof in this section. It is a methodological argument from usul al-fiqh (principles of jurisprudence). The author notes that there are general ('umum) verses promising reward and general verses threatening punishment. When these conflict (ta'arada), the author argues that we give preponderance (tarjih) to the verses of promise by appealing to the specific (khass) verses that indicate pardon ('afw) and forgiveness (ghufran). The reasoning is that the verses of pardon must be specific (because they apply to some people or situations), while the verses of threat are general (applying to all sinners). If the threat verses were also specific, they would not serve the intended purpose of deterrence. When a specific text and a general text conflict in interpretation, the specific takes precedence over the general (al-khass yuqaddam 'ala al-'amm). Therefore, the promise verses are preponderant. The author concludes that with all these proofs, the arguments of the Mu'tazila (who insisted on the preponderance of threat) are completely refuted.

Key Terms
عمومات
الوعد
الوعيد
آيات العفو
خاص
عام
ترجيح
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the argument is circular because it uses verses of pardon to establish preponderance, but Imam Razi is applying a standard hermeneutical principle from usul al-fiqh.
Correction: Imam Razi is not assuming the conclusion; he uses the fact that pardon verses are necessarily specific (not that they are stronger per se) and then applies the rule that specific texts outweigh general ones.

Checkpoint

B) Because they are specific and the threat verses are general
C) Because they were revealed later
D) Because they are easier to understand

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's thirteenth proof shows that verses of forgiveness (specific) supersede general threats, establishing hope over fear.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recall your past sins, does fear of punishment overshadow your hope in God's forgiveness? How does this principle—that specific verses of pardon override general threats—challenge your current state of mind? What one verse of forgiveness will you memorize this week to reinforce hope over fear?
?
If you truly believed that God's mercy (through specific pardon verses) is more decisive than His threats, how would your daily actions and reliance on God change? What is one specific habit you can adopt this week to practically live out this preponderance of hope?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite position that threat dominates. What deep-seated fear in your own heart might reflect that same attitude? How can you actively counter that fear by meditating on a specific verse of pardon (e.g., 'Say: O My servants who have transgressed against themselves, do not despair of the mercy of Allah') and letting it shape your relationship with God today?

The Mu'tazila's First Proof: Curse and Punishment Indicate Deserving Punishment
The Mu'tazila present their first proof for preponderance of threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first proof for the preponderance of threat over promise, based on the consensus that the fasiq is cursed and punished.

Core Idea
The community's treatment of the fasiq proves he deserves punishment, making reward impossible.

The Mu'tazila argue that threat outweighs promise for the grave sinner. Their first proof draws on the consensus practice of cursing and punishing the fasiq. This lesson walks through that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the Mu'tazila's reasoning.
Notice the structure: claim, evidence, conclusion.
2
Identify the first premise
The first premise is that the umma's consensus on cursing and punishing the fasiq proves he deserves punishment. Why? Because if he didn't deserve it, such acts would be unjust.
3
Identify the second premise
The second premise is that punishment and reward are contradictory: one is pure permanent harm, the other pure permanent benefit. They cannot coexist.
This is a logical principle.
4
See the conclusion
Since the fasiq deserves punishment now, he cannot deserve reward. Therefore, the verses of threat take precedence over verses of promise for him.
This is a key Mu'tazili position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة ترجيح جانب الوعيد أولى، ويدل عليه وجوه الأول : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الفاسق يلعن ويحد - على سبيل التنكيل - وذلك يدل على أنه مستحق للعذاب، إذ لو لم يكن مستحقا للعذاب لما جاز لعنه، ولا إقامة الحد عليه - على سبيل التنكيل - وإذا ثبت أنه في هذه الحالة مستحق للعقاب استحال أن يكون مستحقا الثواب؛ لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقهما محالا. وثبت أن استحقاق العقاب حاصل في الحال، فوجب أن يكون استحقاق الثواب غير حاصل في الحال. فثبت بهذا الطريق أن عمومات الوعيد راجحة على عمومات الوعد.
The Mu'tazila said: Preponderance of the side of threat is more appropriate, and it is indicated by proofs. The first: That the community (umma) is agreed that the fasiq is cursed and flogged — by way of punishment — and that indicates that he deserves punishment. For if he were not deserving of punishment, it would not be permissible to curse him nor to administer the prescribed punishment (hadd) on him — by way of punishment. And when it is established that in this state he deserves punishment, it is impossible that he deserves reward, because punishment is a pure, permanent harm, and reward is a pure, permanent benefit, and combining them is impossible. So combining deserving them is impossible. And it is established that deserving punishment is present in the present state, so it must be that deserving reward is not present in the present state. So it is established by this method that the general verses of threat are preponderant over the general verses of promise.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof of the Mu'tazila, who argue for the preponderance of threat (wa'eed) over promise (wa'd) for the fasiq (grave sinner). The proof is based on communal practice (ijma' of the umma) that the fasiq is cursed and subjected to hadd punishments (like flogging for theft or fornication). This shows that he deserves punishment ('adhab). If he deserves punishment, he cannot simultaneously deserve reward, because reward is pure permanent benefit and punishment is pure permanent harm, and they are contradictory. Since he deserves punishment in the present (bi'l-haal), he cannot deserve reward. Therefore, the general verses that threaten punishment must take precedence over those promising reward. This is the first of several proofs they will offer.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
ترجيح
الوعيد
الفاسق
يلعن
يحد
التنكيل
مستحق
العقاب
الثواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Cursing and punishment in this life imply eternal condemnation without possibility of change.
Correction: The Mu'tazila's argument is about present deservingness (bi'l-haal), not necessarily final fate. They use it to argue for the preponderance of threat, but they also allow for repentance.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila's first proof, what is the evidence that the fasiq deserves punishment?
A) Quranic verses about hell
B) The consensus of the umma on cursing and punishing the fasiq
C) The fact that he committed a sin
D) The absence of repentance

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first proof for the preponderance of threat relies on the consensus that the fasiq deserves punishment, which logically excludes deserving reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you honestly believe you currently deserve punishment or reward? How does the Mu'tazila's assertion that you cannot deserve both force you to confront the state of your soul right now?
?
Have you ever spoken harshly about someone else's sin, joining the 'community' in effectively cursing them? What does that action reveal about your own belief in their deservingness—and does it reflect arrogance or a genuine conviction about justice?
?
If, as the Mu'tazila argue, deserving punishment and reward cannot coexist, then where do you stand? Are you living as someone who deserves reward, or are you hoping that your good deeds will cancel out your sins without repentance? What is the honest answer, and what will you do about it today?

Imam Razi's Quranic Rebuttal of the Mu'tazila
Imam Razi cites Quranic evidence for worldly punishments of sinners to rebut the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses Quranic evidence to rebut the Mu'tazila's claim that worldly punishments of the fasiq prove eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Worldly punishments do not dictate eternal fate.

In this lesson, Imam Razi takes on the Mu'tazila's first proof head-on. They argued that because the fasiq is cursed and punished in this world, he must deserve eternal punishment. Razi agrees that such punishments exist—but he provides Quranic evidence to show they are legal penalties in this life, not indicators of final judgment. Read on to see how he turns their own proof into a counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila's claim
Recall that the Mu'tazila argue that the fasiq (grave sinner) is cursed and receives hadd punishments, which they say indicates he deserves eternal hell. Razi accepts the premise but challenges the conclusion.
The dispute is not about whether punishments occur, but about what they mean for the afterlife.
2
Read Razi's rebuttal passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi structures his response by listing four types of worldly punishment and providing a Quranic verse for each.
The verse citations are key—Razi is proving that these punishments are indeed prescribed by God.
3
Identify the four categories of punishment
Razi mentions: curse (la'na), punishment by way of deterrent (tankil), chastisement ('adhab), and humiliation (khizy). Find the corresponding Quranic verses for each.
Each verse explicitly mentions the punishment in this world, not the afterlife.
4
Reflect on Razi's argumentative strategy
Ask yourself: Does the existence of these worldly punishments necessarily prove eternal punishment? Razi's point is that they are legal penalties (hudud) that do not settle the question of final judgment for a believer.
This is a classic Ash'ari move—distinguishing worldly legal punishment from eternal divine justice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه جميع مقدماته ظاهرة إلا قولنا : إن الفاسق يلعن ويقام الحد عليه على سبيل التنكيل، وعلى سبيل العذاب، وعلى سبيل الخزي فنقول : الدليل على أنه يلعن قوله تعالى في قاتل المؤمن: وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ الله [النساء: ﴿أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [هود : ۱۸] والدليل على أنه يقام عليه الحد على سبيل التنكيل، قوله تعالى : ﴿وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جزاء بما كسبا تكلا من الله والله عزيز حكيم [المائدة : ۳۸] والدليل على أنه يحد على سبيل العذاب قوله تعالى في الزانية والزاني : ﴿وَلَيَشْهَدْ عَذَابَهُمَا طَائِفَةٌ مِّنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [النور: ٢] والدليل على أنهم من أهل الخزي قوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق: (إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَمْر [المائدة : ۳۳] إلى قوله: ﴿ذَلِكَ لَهُمْ خِزْيٌ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَلَهُمْ فِي الْآخِرَةِ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمُ [المائدة : ٣٣]
And this proof which we have mentioned — all its premises are apparent except our statement: that the fasiq is cursed and the prescribed punishment (hadd) is established upon him by way of punishment (tankil), and by way of chastisement ('adhab), and by way of humiliation (khizy). So we say: The evidence that he is cursed is His statement, the Exalted, concerning the killer of a believer: 'And Allah became angry with him and cursed him' [an-Nisa' 4:?] (and) 'Indeed, the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' [Hud 11:18]. And the evidence that the prescribed punishment is established upon him by way of punishment is His statement, the Exalted: 'And the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as a recompense for what they earned, a deterrent from Allah, and Allah is Mighty, Wise' [al-Ma'ida 5:38]. And the evidence that he is flogged by way of chastisement is His statement, the Exalted, concerning the fornicator and fornicatress: 'And let a group of the believers witness their punishment' [an-Nur 24:2]. And the evidence that they are among the people of humiliation is His statement, the Exalted, concerning highway robbers: 'Indeed, the recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger...' [al-Ma'ida 5:33] up to His statement: 'That is for them a humiliation in this world, and for them in the Hereafter is a great punishment' [al-Ma'ida 5:33].
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is Imam Razi's rebuttal of the Mu'tazila's first proof. The Mu'tazila had argued that the ulema curse the fasiq and administer hadd punishments, which proves he deserves eternal punishment. Razi responds by deconstructing their premise: the claim that the fasiq is cursed and punished in this world. He cites Quranic evidence to show that such curses and punishments indeed exist, but he does so to demonstrate that these are legal penalties (hudud) in this world, not necessarily indications of eternal damnation. He quotes verses: the curse on the killer of a believer (4:93), the curse on wrongdoers (11:18), the cutting of hands for theft (5:38), the public flogging of fornicators (24:2), and the humiliation of highway robbers (5:33). Razi's point is that these worldly punishments are prescribed by God, but they do not settle the question of eternal reward or punishment for the fasiq who also has faith. The implication is that while the fasiq may be subject to legal penalties in this life, that does not negate the possibility of divine forgiveness in the afterlife. The Mu'tazila's proof is thus based on a premise that does not necessarily support their conclusion.

Correction: Imam Razi shows that these are worldly legal penalties prescribed by God, but they do not determine the final judgment. A fasiq may still have faith and be subject to forgiveness in the Hereafter.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses Quranic evidence to prove that the fasiq is punished in this world, but this does not entail eternal punishment—worldly penalties and final judgment are distinct.

This passage shows that even the most severe worldly penalties do not close the door of mercy. First Mu'tazili Proof: Earthly Punishment Indicates Eternal Threat
Mu'tazila argue from consensus on cursing and punishing the sinner that threat outweighs promise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazilite argument for why verses of threat (wa'id) are stronger than verses of promise (wa'd), based on the communal practice of cursing and punishing sinners in this world.

Core Idea
This-worldly punishment of sinners shows they deserve eternal punishment over reward.

This passage presents the first of several Mu'tazilite proofs for the preponderance of threat (wa'id) over promise (wa'd). The Mu'tazila argue from the consensus of the Muslim community that sinners are cursed and subjected to hadd penalties in this world, concluding that they deserve punishment in the Hereafter and thus threat verses outweigh promise verses. The author then supports the premises with scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazilite claim
Begin by noting that the Mu'tazila assert the preponderance of threat over promise, and they present this as the first of several proofs.
2
Identify the key premise: Ijma' on punishment of the fasiq
Recognize that the argument rests on the consensus (ijma') that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty as a deterrent.
This premise is claimed to be evident except for the part about cursing and punishing, which is then supported by Quranic verses.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the reasoning: if the sinner deserves punishment in the Hereafter (because of worldly punishment), then he cannot also deserve reward, since punishment and reward are opposites (pure perpetual harm vs. pure perpetual benefit).
Combine the logical rule against combining opposites with the establishment of entitlement to punishment.
4
Examine the scriptural evidence
Review the verses cited for cursing, deterrent punishment, chastisement, and disgrace to confirm the premise that sinners are punished in this world.
This evidence is meant to show that the sinner is indeed subject to divine reprimand and worldly penalties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت المعتزلة ترجيح جانب الوعيد أولى، ويدل عليه وجوه الأول : أن الأمة مجمعة على أن الفاسق يلعن ويحد - على سبيل التنكيل - وذلك يدل على أنه مستحق للعذاب، إذ لو لم يكن مستحقا للعذاب لما جاز لعنه، ولا إقامة الحد عليه - على سبيل التنكيل - وإذا ثبت أنه في هذه الحالة مستحق للعقاب استحال أن يكون مستحقا الثواب؛ لأن العقاب مضرة خالصة دائمة، والثواب منفعة خالصة دائمة، والجمع بينهما محال، فكان الجمع بين استحقاقهما محالا. وثبت أن استحقاق العقاب حاصل في الحال، فوجب أن يكون استحقاق الثواب غير حاصل في الحال. فثبت بهذا الطريق أن عمومات الوعيد راجحة على عمومات الوعد. وهذا الدليل الذي ذكرناه جميع مقدماته ظاهرة إلا قولنا : إن الفاسق يلعن ويقام الحد عليه على سبيل التنكيل، وعلى سبيل العذاب، وعلى سبيل الخزي فنقول : الدليل على أنه يلعن قوله تعالى في قاتل المؤمن: وَغَضِبَ اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَلَعَنَهُ الله [النساء: ٩٣] وقوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَا لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [هود : ۱۸] والدليل على أنه يقام عليه الحد على سبيل التنكيل، قوله تعالى : ﴿وَالسَارِقُ وَالسَّارِقَةُ فَأَقْطَعُوا أَيْدِيَهُمَا جزاء بما كسبا تكلا من الله والله عزيز حكيم [المائدة : ۳۸] والدليل على أنه يحد على سبيل العذاب قوله تعالى في الزانية والزاني : ﴿وَلَيَشْهَدْ عَذَابَهُمَا طَائِفَةٌ مِّنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [النور: ٢] والدليل على أنهم من أهل الخزي قوله تعالى في قطاع الطريق: (إِنَّمَا جَزَاؤُا الَّذِينَ يُحَارِبُونَ اللَّهَ وَرَسُولَمْر [المائدة : ۳۳] إلى قوله: ﴿ذَلِكَ لَهُمْ خِزْيٌ فِي الدُّنْيَا وَلَهُمْ فِي الْآخِرَةِ عَذَابٌ عَظِيمُ [المائدة : ٣٣] . فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن استحقاق العذاب حاصل فكان استحقاق الثواب زائلا، وهذا يقتضي ترجيح الوعيد على جانب الوعد. هذا غاية تحقيق كلام المعتزلة في هذا المقام
The Mu'tazila said: giving preponderance to the side of threat is more appropriate, and it is indicated by [several] aspects. The first: that the community (umma) is in consensus that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty—by way of deterrent punishment—and that indicates that he deserves chastisement, for if he did not deserve chastisement, it would not be permissible to curse him nor to establish the hadd penalty against him—by way of deterrent punishment. And when it is established that in this state he deserves punishment, it is impossible that he deserves reward, because punishment is a pure perpetual harm and reward is a pure perpetual benefit, and combining them is impossible, so combining their entitlement is impossible. And it is established that entitlement to punishment is present in the current state, so it must be that entitlement to reward is not present in the current state. Thus, by this way it is established that the general texts of threat are preponderant over the general texts of promise. And this argument that we have mentioned—all its premises are evident except our statement: 'the sinner is cursed and the hadd penalty is established against him by way of deterrent punishment, by way of chastisement, and by way of disgrace.' So we say: the evidence that he is cursed is His saying, the Exalted, regarding the killer of a believer: 'And Allah became angry with him and cursed him' [an-Nisa 4:93] and His saying: 'Surely the curse of Allah is upon the wrongdoers' [Hud 11:18]. And the evidence that the hadd penalty is established against him by way of deterrent punishment is His saying: 'As for the thief, male and female, cut off their hands as a recompense for what they have earned, a deterrent from Allah, and Allah is Almighty, Wise' [al-Ma'ida 5:38]. And the evidence that he is punished by way of chastisement is His saying, regarding the adulterer and adulteress: 'And let a group of believers witness their punishment' [an-Nur 24:2]. And the evidence that they are among the people of disgrace is His saying, regarding highway robbers: 'The recompense of those who wage war against Allah and His Messenger... up to His saying: 'That is for them a disgrace in this world, and for them in the Hereafter a great punishment' [al-Ma'ida 5:33]. So it is established by these considerations that entitlement to punishment is present, so entitlement to reward is absent. And this necessitates giving preponderance to threat over the side of promise. This is the ultimate verification of the Mu'tazila's statement in this context.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage presents the first Mu'tazilite proof for the preponderance of threat verses over promise verses. It is a multi-step argument. The Mu'tazila begin by asserting that the Muslim community is in consensus that the sinner (fasiq) is cursed and subjected to the hadd penalty as a deterrent. This indicates that he deserves punishment in the Hereafter, because if he did not deserve punishment, it would not be permissible to curse him or impose the hadd as a deterrent. Then they argue that since he deserves punishment in the present state, he cannot also deserve reward, because punishment is pure perpetual harm and reward is pure perpetual benefit, and their combination is impossible. Therefore, entitlement to reward is negated. Thus, the general texts of threat are stronger. After stating the argument, Razi then provides scriptural evidence for the premises that the sinner is cursed and punished in this world. He cites verses on murder, theft, adultery, and highway robbery to show that these entail curse, deterrent, chastisement, and disgrace. He concludes that the Mu'tazilite position is fully established.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila deny that God can forgive sinners.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny forgiveness per se; they argue that the evidential weight of this-worldly punishment shows that threat is the stronger indicator.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the Mu'tazilite argument in this passage?
A) The sinner deserves reward in the Hereafter.
B) The consensus of the community is that the sinner is cursed and punished in this world.
C) Verses of promise are more numerous.
D) God's mercy is general for all sinners.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use the fact that sinners are cursed and punished in this world as evidence that they deserve eternal punishment, making verses of threat outweigh those of promise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe a sinner being punished in this world—whether by law or by consequence—does it make you feel that God's threat is more real to you than His promise? How does that feeling affect your hope in repentance?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that if punishment is deserved, reward cannot be also deserved. Have you ever felt that a past sin has disqualified you from receiving God's mercy? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God's promise can still be yours despite your faults?
?
This lesson uses the community's consensus on cursing the sinner as evidence. When you hear others curse a sinful person, do you join them? Or do you hold back, remembering that only God knows the final outcome? What does your reaction reveal about your own heart regarding hope and fear?

Second Reason: Distinguishing Shirk Becomes Useless
Razi's second reason: a conditional argument against interpreting the verse as forgiveness for the deserving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second reason why Quran 4:48 must refer to the major sinner before repentance, based on the uselessness of distinguishing shirk from other sins if the verse applied to those deserving forgiveness.

Core Idea
If forgiveness were for the deserving, distinguishing shirk is pointless.

In this passage, Razi presents the second of three arguments within his fourth proof. He points out that if the verse meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like the repentant), then there would be no point in singling out shirk. This argument exposes a logical inconsistency in the alternative interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation for the second reason. Notice how Razi sets up a conditional: 'if the verse meant X, then Y follows.'
2
Analyze the conditional
Identify the premise (the verse means forgiveness for the deserving) and the consequence (distinguishing shirk is useless). Ask: why is this a problem for the opponent?
3
Understand the force of the argument
This is an ilzam—a necessary consequence that the opponent must accept. Razi is showing that the opponent's position leads to an absurdity.
This argument is built on Mu'tazilite premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون وثانيها : أنه لو كان قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يَشَاءُ) [النساء : ٤٨] معناه أنه يغفر لمن يستحق المغفرة كالتائبين وأصحاب الصغائر ، لم يبق لتمييز الشرك عن غيره فائدة ؛ لأنه تعالى يغفر ما دون الشرك لمن يستحق العقاب ولا يغفر لمن يستحق العقاب، فكذلك يغفر الشرك لمن لا يستحق العقاب ولا يغفره لمن يستحق العقاب فلا يبقى البتة لهذا التقسيم فائدة. وثالثها: أن غفران صاحب الكبيرة وصاحب الصغيرة بعد التوبة واجب والواجب لا يجوز تعليقه على المشيئة؛ لأن المعلق على المشيئة هو الذي إن شاء فعله و إن شاء تركه والواجب هو الذي لا بد من فعله شاء أم أبي، والمغفرة المذكورة في الآية معلقة على المشيئة فلا يجوز أن يكون المراد منها هو المغفرة للتائب، وصاحب الصغيرة. واعلم أن هذه الوجوه بأسرها إلزامات مبنية على قول المعتزلة : إنه يجب الغفران للتائب، وصاحب الصغيرة ونحن لا نقول بذلك.
The thirty-fifth issue. And the second: that if His saying, Exalted is He: 'And He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant that He forgives those who deserve forgiveness, like the repentant and those with minor sins, then there would be no benefit in distinguishing shirk from other sins. For He, Exalted is He, forgives what is less than shirk for those who deserve punishment, and He does not forgive those who deserve punishment; similarly, He forgives shirk for those who do not deserve punishment, and He does not forgive it for those who deserve punishment, so this division would have no benefit at all. And the third: that the forgiveness of the major sinner and the minor sinner after repentance is obligatory, and what is obligatory cannot be attached to the will, because what is attached to the will is that which if He wills He does it and if He wills He leaves it, while the obligatory is that which must be done whether He wills or not, and the forgiveness mentioned in the verse is attached to the will, so it cannot be that the intended meaning is the forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner. And know that all these points are necessary consequences built upon the statement of the Mu'tazila that forgiveness is obligatory for the repentant and the minor sinner, and we do not say that.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third reasons within the fourth proof, along with a note about their basis. The second reason: if the verse 'He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like repentants and minor sinners), then distinguishing shirk from other sins becomes pointless. Why? Because if forgiveness is given to those who deserve punishment for minor sins, and denied to those who deserve punishment for shirk, then the same logic would apply: shirk would be forgiven for those who do not deserve punishment (after repentance) and not for those who do. So the division would have no benefit. The third reason: forgiveness of the repentant and the minor sinner is obligatory, but what is obligatory cannot be attached to God's will (mashi'a). The verse explicitly attaches forgiveness to 'whom He wills', so it cannot refer to obligatory forgiveness. Finally, the author notes that these arguments are necessary consequences (ilzamāt) based on the Mu'tazilite premise that forgiveness is obligatory for the repentant and minor sinner; however, Razi states that he (as an Ash'ari) does not hold that view.

Key Terms
تمييز الشرك
فائدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Razi is directly arguing that forgiveness is only for the undeserving.
Correction: Razi's point is dialectical: he shows that the opponent's logic leads to absurdity. He is not asserting his own view here.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's second reason, if the verse meant forgiveness for those who deserve forgiveness, what would be the consequence?
A) Shirk would be unforgivable
B) Distinguishing shirk from other sins would have no benefit
C) The verse would only apply to repentant
D) God's will would be limited

Key Takeaway
Razi's second reason is a conditional argument that shows the alternative interpretation leads to the absurdity that the verse's distinction between shirk and other sins is useless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that if forgiveness were based on desert, the distinction between shirk and other sins would be pointless. How does this challenge your own tendency to think you 'deserve' forgiveness because of your good deeds? When have you felt entitled to Allah's mercy, and how can you shift your heart to see it as pure grace?
?
The verse says Allah does not forgive shirk but forgives what is less than that for whom He wills. Does your life reflect the gravity of shirk? In what subtle ways do you prioritize people's approval over Allah's, and what will you do today to identify and uproot those hidden forms of shirk?
?
Razi's argument warns against imposing human logic on divine speech. Where in your faith do you find yourself rationalizing away clear commands or reinterpretations to suit your desires? Commit to one specific verse or command you have been avoiding and decide how you will submit to it this week.

Third Reason: Obligatory Forgiveness Cannot Depend on Will
Razi's third reason: the incompatibility of obligatory forgiveness with the verse's attachment to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third reason why Quran 4:48 must refer to the major sinner before repentance: because forgiveness of the repentant is considered obligatory, but the verse attaches forgiveness to God's will, which is incompatible with obligation.

Core Idea
Obligatory forgiveness cannot be attached to God's will.

Here Razi presents the third argument. He states that if forgiveness of the repentant and minor sinner is obligatory upon God (as the Mu'tazila claim), then it cannot be conditional on His will. However, the verse explicitly says 'for whom He wills,' indicating that forgiveness is not obligatory. Thus, the verse must refer to those whom God chooses to forgive out of His free will—namely, the major sinner before repentance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the third reason. Focus on the logic: the forgiveness mentioned in the verse is conditional on will, but forgiveness of the repentant is wajib, which is unconditional.
2
Define 'wajib' and 'mashi'a'
Understand that in kalam, 'wajib' means something that must happen, while 'mashi'a' means something that can be done or not. The two concepts are contradictory.
3
Recognize the sectarian context
Razi notes that this argument is an ilzam based on Mu'tazilite premises. As an Ash'ari, he does not hold that forgiveness is obligatory upon God.
This shows Razi's dialectical method: using the opponent's own assumptions against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And know that all these points are necessary consequences built upon the statement of the Mu'tazila that forgiveness is obligatory for the repentant and the minor sinner, and we do not say that.

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third reasons within the fourth proof, along with a note about their basis. The second reason: if the verse 'He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' meant forgiveness for those who deserve it (like repentants and minor sinners), then distinguishing shirk from other sins becomes pointless. Fourth Reason: Forgiveness for All Except Shirk
Razi states the fourth reason and its scriptural basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth reason Razi relies upon from Quran 4:48, which establishes that Allah forgives all sins except shirk for some people.

Core Idea
The verse 'forgives what is less than that' gives certainty that Allah forgives all except shirk.

This lesson presents the fourth and most relied-upon reason from Imam Razi's discussion of Quran 4:48. The reason builds on the certainty (qat') derived from the verse's wording to argue that Allah forgives all sins except shirk, both minor and major, before and after repentance. It then explains how the phrase 'for whom He wills' restricts this to some people, not all. This argument is central to the theological debate on the fate of grave sinners.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice how Razi emphasizes 'upon it is reliance'.
The passage is concise; every phrase matters.
2
Identify the two key phrases
Find the two parts of the verse Razi uses: 'and He forgives what is less than that' and 'for whom He wills'. Understand how each contributes to the argument.
3
Understand the derivation of certainty
Razi claims the verse gives certainty (qat'). Ask yourself why he says that and what it implies for the scope of forgiveness.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : . وعليه الاعتماد - أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ الله [النساء : ٤٨] يفيد القطع بأنه تعالى يغفر كل ما سوى الشرك، وذلك يندرج فيه الصغيرة والكبيرة بعد التوبة وقبلها، ثم قوله بعد ذلك : لِمَن يَشَاء يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام لكن لا للكل بل للبعض، فثبت أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام للبعض، وذلك هو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لا نسلم ان المغفرة عبارة عن أن لا يعذب العصاة في الآخرة، بيانه : أن المغفرة إسقاط العذاب، والمفهوم من إسقاط العذاب أعم من إسقاطه دائما، أو لا دائما، واللفظ الموضوع بإزاء القدر المشترك لا إشعار فيه لكل واحد من ذينك القيدين، فإذن لفظ المغفرة لا دلالة فيه على كون الإسقاط دائماً. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقوبة الشرك عن الدنيا، لكن يؤخر ما دون الشرك عن الدنيا في حق من يشاء ؟ لا يقال : كيف يصح عقاب الدنيا على المؤمنين ؟ هذا التأويل ونحن لا نشاهد البتة اليوم مزية للكفار في لأنا نقول : تقدير الآية أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقاب الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، ويؤخر عقاب ما دون الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، فحصل بذلك تخويف كلا الفريقين ؛ لأن الكفار والفساق يخافون أن يعجل عقابهم في الدنيا، وإن كان لا يفعل
And the fourth—and upon it is reliance—that His saying, Exalted is He: 'And He forgives what is less than that' gives certainty that He, Exalted is He, forgives everything besides shirk, and that includes the minor and major sin after repentance and before it. Then His saying after that: 'for whom He wills' indicates that He, Exalted is He, forgives all these categories, but not for everyone, rather for some. So it is established that He, Exalted is He, forgives all these categories for some, and that is the desired conclusion. If it is said: We do not accept that 'forgiveness' is an expression for not punishing sinners in the Hereafter. Explanation: forgiveness is the removal of punishment, and the understanding from removal of punishment is more general than removing it permanently or not permanently, and the word placed for the common meaning does not indicate either of these two restrictions. So then the word 'forgiveness' does not indicate that the removal is permanent. If this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible that the intended meaning is that God, Exalted is He, does not delay the punishment of shirk from this world, but He delays the punishment of what is less than shirk from this world for whom He wills? It cannot be said: How can there be worldly punishment for believers? This interpretation—and we do not at all observe today any advantage for the disbelievers— because we say: The estimation of the verse is that God, Exalted is He, does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world for whom He wills, and He delays the punishment of what is less than shirk in this world for whom He wills. Thus by that, both groups are frightened; because the disbelievers and the wicked fear that their punishment may be hastened in this world, even if He does not do it.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth reason, which Razi says is the one relied upon, and then an objection with its answer. The fourth reason: The phrase 'and He forgives what is less than that' gives certainty (qat') that God forgives everything except shirk, including both minor and major sins, before and after repentance. Then 'for whom He wills' indicates that He forgives these categories for some people, not all. Thus, God forgives these sins for some people—the desired conclusion. The objection: The opponent denies that forgiveness means not punishing sinners in the Hereafter. He argues that forgiveness is simply removal of punishment, regardless of permanence. The word 'forgiveness' does not specify permanent removal. Therefore, why not interpret the verse as meaning that God does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world (i.e., He punishes it promptly), but He delays the punishment of lesser sins in this world for whom He wills? That is, forgiveness here means deferring worldly punishment, not removing Hellfire eternally. The answer: The objector says we do not see any worldly advantage for disbelievers today (so how can this interpretation hold?). The response: The meaning is that God, may He be exalted, does not delay the punishment of shirk in this world for whom He wills (i.e., He may punish some disbelievers immediately), and He delays the punishment of lesser sins in this world for whom He wills. This causes fear for both groups: disbelievers and sinners fear that their punishment may be hastened in this world, even if He does not actually do it. Thus, the interpretation is plausible as a threat.

Correction: The phrase 'for whom He wills' shows it is only for some, not all.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's fourth reason, what does the verse 'and He forgives what is less than that' provide?

B) Certainty
C) A suggestion
D) A conditional

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have committed sins you consider major. Does the certainty from this verse that Allah forgives all except shirk give you genuine hope, or do you still doubt His mercy? How does your belief in Allah's will impact your repentance?
?
Razi emphasizes that forgiveness is for some, not all. What distinguishes those whom Allah wills to forgive from those He does not? Is your relationship with Allah such that you can hope to be among those He forgives, or do you take His mercy for granted?
?
The verse says 'for whom He wills'—are you content with leaving your forgiveness entirely to Allah's will, or do you try to earn it through deeds? What would it mean for you to rely solely on His mercy without presumption?

Objection and Answer: Forgiveness as Worldly Deferral
Objection and answer regarding the meaning of forgiveness in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that forgiveness in Quran 4:48 could mean deferring worldly punishment, and Razi's response that this interpretation still serves as a threat.

Core Idea
Forgiveness does not necessarily mean eternal pardon; it could be deferring punishment in this world.

In response to Razi's fourth reason, an opponent raises an objection that challenges the meaning of 'forgiveness' (maghfirah). The opponent argues that the word simply means removal of punishment, without specifying whether it is permanent or temporary. Therefore, the verse could be interpreted as Allah not delaying the punishment of shirk in this world but delaying the punishment of lesser sins for some people. This lesson presents the objection and Razi's answer, which defends the original interpretation by showing that even this reinterpretation yields a meaningful threat.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The objection is a complex linguistic argument; read it twice if needed.
Pay attention to the logic of the objection.
2
Analyze the linguistic point
The objector says 'forgiveness' is neutral regarding permanence. Understand why this is key to the alternative interpretation.
This is a typical kalam move.
3
Examine the alternative interpretation
The objector proposes that the verse means Allah does not delay punishment of shirk in this world but delays lesser sins. Consider what this implies.
4
Understand Razi's response
Razi answers that even this interpretation works as a threat to both disbelievers and sinners. Connect this to the purpose of the verse.
This shows the resilience of the proof.

ورابعها : . وعليه الاعتماد - أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ الله [النساء : ٤٨] يفيد القطع بأنه تعالى يغفر كل ما سوى الشرك، وذلك يندرج فيه الصغيرة والكبيرة بعد التوبة وقبلها، ثم قوله بعد ذلك : لِمَن يَشَاء يدل على أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام لكن لا للكل بل للبعض، فثبت أنه تعالى يغفر كل هذه الأقسام للبعض، وذلك هو المطلوب. فإن قيل : لا نسلم ان المغفرة عبارة عن أن لا يعذب العصاة في الآخرة، بيانه : أن المغفرة إسقاط العذاب، والمفهوم من إسقاط العذاب أعم من إسقاطه دائما، أو لا دائما، واللفظ الموضوع بإزاء القدر المشترك لا إشعار فيه لكل واحد من ذينك القيدين، فإذن لفظ المغفرة لا دلالة فيه على كون الإسقاط دائماً. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون المراد أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقوبة الشرك عن الدنيا، لكن يؤخر ما دون الشرك عن الدنيا في حق من يشاء ؟ لا يقال : كيف يصح عقاب الدنيا على المؤمنين ؟ هذا التأويل ونحن لا نشاهد البتة اليوم مزية للكفار في لأنا نقول : تقدير الآية أن الله تعالى لا يؤخر عقاب الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، ويؤخر عقاب ما دون الشرك في الدنيا لمن يشاء، فحصل بذلك تخويف كلا الفريقين ؛ لأن الكفار والفساق يخافون أن يعجل عقابهم في الدنيا، وإن كان لا يفعل

The Seventh Proof: God's Forgiveness and Repentance
Razi cites and defends the verse 'يغفر الذنوب جميعا' as a proof for temporary punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's use of the verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' as a proof for temporary punishment and how he defends it against objections through conditional interpretation and eschatological promise.

Core Idea
The verse promises future forgiveness after repentance and punishment, not unconditional amnesty.

Razi now presents the seventh proof for the cessation of punishment for sinful believers. He cites the Qur'anic verse which seems to promise universal forgiveness. An objector points out that this contradicts his position. Razi meets the challenge by reinterpreting the verse as conditional upon repentance and reframing it as a future promise.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation once to get the flow of the argument before delving into analysis.
Pay attention to the structure: objection, concession, two reasons, and final answer.
2
Identify the proof and objection
Find where Razi presents the seventh proof and where the objector challenges it. Notice how Razi concedes the apparent contradiction before reinterpreting.
3
Analyze the two reasons and final answer
Examine the two reasons Razi gives for interpreting the verse with repentance, and his final answer that the verse is a future promise of forgiveness for all believers.
Consider how each reason addresses the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الخامسة والثلاثون
فإن عارضوا بآيات الوعيد قلنا : الترجيح ما تقدم ولما كانت تلك الترجيحات ظنية رجونا العفو، وما قطعنا به.
لا جرم و جرم
الحجة السادسة : أنا سنقيم الدلالة إن شاء الله تعالى على أن تأثير شفاعة محمد ال في إسقاط العقاب، وذلك يدل على قولنا في هذه المسألة. الحجة السابعة : قوله تعالى : هو إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَمِيعاً) [الزمر : ٥٣] . فإن قيل : هذه الآية دلت على القطع بالمغفرة لكل العصاة و أنتم لا تقولون بهذا المذهب فما تدل عليه الآية لا تقولون ،به وما تقولون به لا تدل الآية عليه سلمنا ذلك، لكن المراد أنه تعالى يغفر جميع الذنوب مع التوبة، وحمل الآية على هذا المعنى أولى لوجهين : الأول : أنا إذا حملناها على هذا الوجه فقد حملناها على جميع الذنوب - على ما هو ظاهر الآية - وإن حملناها على ما ذكرتم لا يمكن حمله على ظاهر الآية ؛ لأنه تعالى لا يغفر جميع الذنوب من غير توبة فإن الكفر ذنب والله لا يغفره إلا مع التوبة. الثاني : أنه تعالى ذكر عقيب هذه الآية قوله تعالى: وَأَنبُوا إِلَى رَبِّكُمْ وَأَسْلِمُوا لَهُ مِن قَبْلِ أَن يَأْتِيَكُمُ الْعَذَابُ ﴾ [الزمر : ٥٤] والإنابة التوبة. والجواب عن الأول: أن قوله: ﴿يَغْفِرُ الذُّنُوبَ جَميعاً وعد منه تعالى بأنه سيفعل ذلك في المستقبل، ونحن نقطع بذلك، فإنا قاطعون بأنه تعالى سيخرج كل المؤمنين من النار لا محالة، فيكون هذا قطعًا لحصول الغفران لا محالة وبهذا الطريق يظهر أنه لا حاجة في إجراء الآية على ظاهرها إلى إضمار قبل التوبة، والله أعلم.
The thirty-fifth issue. If they object with verses of threat, we say: the preference is what preceded, and since those preferences were speculative, we hope for pardon, and we are certain of what we have affirmed. Without doubt. The sixth proof: We will present the evidence, God willing, that the intercession of Muhammad ﷺ has an effect in removing punishment, and that supports our position on this issue. The seventh proof: His statement, Exalted is He: 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' (Az-Zumar 53). If it is said: This verse indicates certainty of forgiveness for every sinner, and you do not hold this doctrine, so what the verse indicates you do not say, and what you say the verse does not indicate. We accept that, but the intended meaning is that He, Exalted is He, forgives all sins with repentance, and interpreting the verse in this sense is preferable for two reasons: First, if we interpret it in this way, we have applied it to all sins—according to the apparent meaning of the verse—and if we interpret it according to what you mentioned, it cannot be applied to the apparent meaning of the verse, because He, Exalted is He, does not forgive all sins without repentance, for disbelief is a sin and God does not forgive it except with repentance. Second, because He, Exalted is He, mentioned after this verse His statement: 'And turn in repentance to your Lord and submit to Him before there comes to you the punishment' (Az-Zumar 54), and turning in repentance is repentance. The answer to the first point: His statement 'He forgives all sins' is a promise from Him, Exalted is He, that He will do that in the future, and we are certain of that, for we are certain that He, Exalted is He, will surely bring out all believers from the Fire without fail, so this is certainty of obtaining forgiveness without fail, and by this way it appears that there is no need in applying the verse according to its apparent meaning to imply 'before repentance.' And God knows best.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage is a proof and defense of the Ash'ari position that punishment for sinful believers is temporary. Razi begins by responding to an objection based on verses of threat (وعيد) that imply eternal punishment. He says the preference is what we have already established (presumably earlier proofs), and since those proofs were speculative, we hope for pardon but are certain of our position. Then he mentions the sixth proof (which is only referenced, not detailed) about the Prophet's intercession mitigating punishment. The main focus is the seventh proof from the Qur'anic verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins.' An objection is raised: this verse seems to grant unconditional forgiveness to all sinners, which contradicts the Ash'ari view that some sinners will be temporarily punished. The objector says that Razi neither accepts what the verse apparently says nor does the verse support what Razi says. Razi concedes the point but reinterprets the verse as meaning forgiveness after repentance. He gives two reasons: First, interpreting with repentance applies to all sins (including disbelief) as the apparent wording suggests, whereas interpreting without repentance would exclude disbelief since God does not forgive disbelief except with repentance. Second, the following verse commands repentance, indicating that the forgiveness is conditioned on repentance. Then Razi answers the first objection (perhaps the initial objection about the verse not applying to him) by saying that the verse is a promise of future forgiveness—God will eventually forgive all believers by removing them from the Fire. Thus, the verse does not require an implicit condition 'before repentance' because the forgiveness is eschatological. The passage ends with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
وعيد
شفاعة
مغفرة
توبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins' means unconditional forgiveness for any sinner regardless of repentance or punishment.
Correction: Razi explains that the verse is conditional upon repentance (for disbelief) and is a future promise for believers that will be fulfilled after any necessary punishment.

Checkpoint
What is the second reason Razi gives for interpreting the verse with repentance?
A) The verse mentions all sins.
B) The following verse commands repentance.
C) The verse is a future promise.
D) The verse excludes disbelief.

Key Takeaway
Razi defends the Ash'ari position by interpreting the verse as a conditional promise that will be fulfilled after repentance and temporary punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'Indeed, Allah forgives all sins,' do you assume you are automatically forgiven without changing your life? How does Razi's insistence on repentance challenge your default attitude? What specific sin are you delaying repenting from right now?
?
Razi argues that the verse is a future promise for believers after punishment. Does that certainty make you more careless about sin or more motivated to repent? Examine your heart: are you using God's mercy as an excuse to keep sinning?
?
The verse is followed immediately by a command to turn back to God. Have you ever experienced a moment where a promise of mercy in the Quran was directly tied to a call to action? What would it look like for you to 'turn in repentance' today, not just in general but from a specific habit you know displeases Him?
